Buying a Father with Intention of Expiation Does Not Count in Hanafi
Inter-madhhab dispute over cause vs. condition in expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that buying a father with intention of expiation does not suffice, and evaluate the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Intention must accompany the cause of manumission, not its condition; purchase is only a condition.

This lesson examines a classic ikhtilaf: if a man buys his own father intending to fulfill an expiation (kafarah), does the manumission count? The Hanafi school says no; the cause (illah) of expiation is the oath, and intention was made at purchase, which is only a condition (shart). Zufar (a Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i say yes, because they consider kinship as the cause. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish cause from condition in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the precise ruling: buying a father with intention of expiation does NOT suffice; the expiation remains unfulfilled.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (an early Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i hold that it DOES suffice. They differ on the underlying cause (illah) of manumission.
Zufar's view is an intra-Hanafi variant; together with Shafi'i they form the opposing camp.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Opponents argue that kinship (qarabah) is the cause of manumission, and purchase is merely a condition. Since intention is present at the time of purchase (the condition) and the cause (kinship) is already present, the intention is effectively concurrent with the cause.
They rely on the hadith: 'A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him as a slave, buys him, and frees him.' They interpret the fa' (then) as sequential but not exclusive.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that the hadith's fa' indicates that the purchase itself is the cause of manumission, not just a condition. The phrase 'fa-yu'tiqu' means the very act of purchase constitutes the freeing, analogous to 'he gave him to drink and quenched his thirst'—one action produces the result.
Hanafis distinguish between 'illah (cause) and shart (condition). Intention must be with the cause (the oath), not with the condition (purchase).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, if a Hanafi buys his father intending expiation, he must still perform another valid expiation. According to Zufar/Shafi'i, it would be satisfied. This affects fatwa in various expiation scenarios (oath, zihar, killing).
The dispute also applies to freeing a umm walad or a relative acquired by inheritance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسقط بشراء أبيه (۳) قوله : وتسقط ... الخ؛ هذه المسألة وإن كان الأليق ذكرها في بحث كفارة اليمين أو كفارة الظهار لكن ذكروها هنا لمناسبة تعليق العتق بالشراء ؛ فإنّه يمين ; ولذا ذكر في «الهداية»(4) قبيل هذه المسألة مسألة ما لو قال : إن اشتريت فلاناً فهو حرّ، فاشتراه ينوي به كفارة يمينه لم يجزه عن الكفّارة. ووجهه بما حاصله : إنّ شرط الخروج عن عهدة التكفير قران نية التكفير بعلة العتق ، وهي اليمين هاهنا ولم يوجد ، وإنّما وجد عند الشراء، وهو شرط العتق لا علته فلا يكون مفيداً حتى لو كانت النيّة مقارنةً لليمن أجزأه عن الكفارة. كذا في «العناية)  (5) كتاب الأيمان اليمين في العتق والطلاق لكفارته هي) : أي الكفارة، هذا عندنا ، وأما عند زُفـر والشَّافِعِي) لا تسقط، فالحاصل أن النيَّةَ لا بُدَّ أن تكونَ مقارنةً لعلة العتق ، فهما جعلا القرابة علة العتق، والملك" شرطاً، ونحن جعلنا على العكس؛ لأنَّ الشَّرع جعل شراء القريب إعتاقاً ؛ فإذا اشترى أباه بنيَّةِ الكفارة كانت النية مقارنةً لعلة العتق خطأ. [1] قوله : لكفارته ؛ أي كفّارة كانت ككفارة اليمين وكفارة الظهار وكفارة القتل [۲] قوله : مقارنة ؛ لعلّة العتق بشرط أن تكون العلّة اختيارية، كشراء القريب، وأن يكون رق المعتق كاملاً ، وعلى هذه القاعدة تتفرّع عدم إجزاء العتق عن الكفارة في صورة اليمين كما مر آنفاً تفصيله ، وسيذكره المصنف و الشارح ، وعدم إجزاء عتق أم الولد على ما سيأتي، وعدم إجزاء عتق ذي رحم محرم عليه بملكه بالإرث ، فإنّ العلة هناك جبرية لا اختيارية ؛ لما تقرّر في موضعه أنّ الإرث جبري لا يسقط بالإسقاط. [٣] قوله : علّة العتق والملك ؛ وذلك لأنّ الشراء إثبات الملك، والإعتاق إزالته، وبينهما منافاة فلا يكون الشراء علّة للعتق بل هو شرط، والعلة القرابة المحرمية، فإذا شرى القريب بنية الكفّارة لم يجز عنها ؛ لعدم اقتران النية بالعلة، بل بالشرط. [٤]قوله : على العكس؛ فعندهما شراء القريب علة للعتق ، والقرابة شرط يدلّ عليه حديث : «لن يجزي ولد والده إلا أن يجده مملوكا فيشتريه فيعتقه»، أخرجه مسلم وأصحاب السنن وإليه أشار الشارح الله بقوله : لأنّ الشرع جعل شراء القريب إعتاقاً. فإن قلت : قد عطف في الحديث الإعتاق على الشراء بحـرف الـفـاء المقتضي للتراخي، ولو بلا مهلة فلا يدلّ على أن الإعتاق هو الشراء. قلت : هو مثل قولهم : سقاه فأرواه ؛ أي بذلك الفعل لا بغيره. كذا في شروح «الهداية»(۳).
And it falls by the purchase of his father. (3) His saying: 'and it falls' etc.; this issue, although it would be more appropriate to mention it in the discussion of expiation of an oath or expiation of zihar, they mentioned it here because of the relevance of suspending manumission on purchase; for it is an oath. Hence, in al-Hidayah before this issue, the issue of if someone said: 'If I buy so-and-so, he is free,' and he buys it intending thereby the expiation of his oath, it does not suffice for the expiation. The explanation is: the condition for exiting the liability of expiation is the conjunction of the intention of expiation with the cause of manumission, which is the oath here, and it did not occur; rather it occurred at the purchase, which is the condition of manumission, not its cause, so it is not beneficial. Even if the intention were concurrent with the oath, it would suffice for the expiation. As in al-Inayah. Book of Oaths: The Oath concerning Manumission and Divorce. His saying: 'for his expiation' i.e., the expiation. This is with us. But according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, it does not fall. The conclusion is that the intention must be concurrent with the cause of manumission. They made kinship the cause of manumission, and ownership a condition; and we made it the opposite. Because the Law made the purchase of a relative a manumission. So if he buys his father with the intention of expiation, the intention would be concurrent with the cause of manumission—this is wrong. [1] His saying: 'for his expiation' i.e., any expiation, such as expiation of an oath, expiation of zihar, and expiation of killing. [2] His saying: 'concurrent' with the cause of manumission, provided that the cause is voluntary, like the purchase of a relative, and that the slave being freed is a full slave. On this principle branch the non-sufficiency of manumission for expiation in the case of the oath as detailed earlier, which the author and commentator will mention; also the non-sufficiency of freeing a umm walad as will come; also the non-sufficiency of freeing a mahram relative acquired by inheritance, for the cause there is involuntary, not voluntary, as established in its place that inheritance is obligatory and cannot be dropped by waiver. [3] His saying: 'the cause of manumission and ownership' because purchase establishes ownership, and manumission removes it, and there is contradiction between them, so purchase is not a cause for manumission but a condition; the cause is the prohibited kinship. Thus if he buys a relative with intention of expiation, it does not suffice because the intention was not concurrent with the cause, but with the condition. [4] His saying: 'on the contrary'; for according to them, purchase of a relative is the cause for manumission, and kinship is a condition. This is indicated by the hadith: 'A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him as a slave, buys him, and frees him.' Narrated by Muslim and the authors of the Sunan. The commentator referred to it by saying: 'because the Law made the purchase of a relative a manumission.' If you say: In the hadith, freeing is conjoined to purchase with the letter fa' indicating succession, and without delay, so it does not indicate that freeing is the purchase. I say: It is like their saying: 'He gave him to drink and quenched his thirst' i.e., by that very action, not by something else. As in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. It discusses the ruling that if a person buys his father with the intention of fulfilling an expiation (kafarah), does that suffice? According to the Hanafi school, it does not suffice; the expiation is not fulfilled. The reason is that the cause (illah) of manumission for expiation is the oath itself, not the purchase. The intention (niyyah) must be concurrent with the cause. In this scenario, the intention is made at the time of purchase, which is only a condition (shart) for manumission, not the cause. Therefore, it is ineffective. The commentator clarifies that this applies to any expiation (oath, zihar, killing). In contrast, Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that it does suffice because they consider the kinship (qarabah) between buyer and slave to be the cause of manumission, and the purchase as a condition. The Hanafi position is supported by the hadith 'A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him as a slave, buys him, and frees him.' The commentator explains that the hadith indicates that the purchase itself is the cause of manumission, not just a condition, by analogy with the expression 'he gave him to drink and quenched his thirst,' meaning the act itself accomplished the result. The passage also highlights the principle that the cause must be voluntary; if it is involuntary (like inheritance), it does not suffice.

Key Terms
علة (illah): The cause or effective reason that triggers a legal ruling. Here, the cause of manumission for expiation is the oath (in Hanafi) or kinship (in Zufar/Shafi'i).
شرط (shart): A condition that must be present for the ruling to apply, but it is not the cause itself. Here, purchase is a condition for manumission, not the cause.
نية (niyyah): Intention. For expiation, the intention must be concurrent with the cause of the act (the oath/expiation cause).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does buying a father with intention of expiation not suffice?
A) Because the father is not a valid slave to be freed.
B) Because the intention must be concurrent with the cause (the oath), not with the condition (purchase).
C) Because the hadith explicitly forbids buying relatives for expiation.
D) Because the purchase creates a conflict of interest.

Key Takeaway
Intention must accompany the cause of a legal act, not its mere condition; this rule determines the validity of expiation via purchase of a relative.

Intention for Emancipation in Expiation: Cause vs. Condition
Dispute on whether intention must accompany cause or condition of emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on when intention must accompany the cause (illa) versus the condition (shart) of emancipation for kaffara, and evaluate the opposing views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Intention must accompany the essence of the cause, not just its activation.

The validity of emancipation as expiation depends on a subtle but crucial distinction between the cause ('illa) and the condition (shart). This lesson unpacks a intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab dispute about whether purchasing a relative with the intention of kaffara suffices. We will trace the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, the objections raised, and the refined response that solidifies the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling according to Abu Hanifa in the two scenarios: purchasing a relative, and swearing to free a slave if purchased. Determine what is the cause and what is the condition.
The core ruling: intention must be concurrent with the 'illa, not the shart.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (a Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i hold the opposite: purchase is the 'illa, kinship is the shart. State their outcome for the same scenarios.
Their view leads to the intention at purchase being sufficient.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith they cite: 'A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him a slave and buys him and emancipates him.' Note how the particle 'fa' is interpreted.
They also rely on the usul principle that suspension prevents causality until the condition occurs.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how Abu Hanifa refutes the opposing interpretation: purchase establishes ownership while emancipation removes it, so they cannot be cause. Instead, kinship is the cause. Also, address the objection based on ta'liq using Ibn Kamal Pasha's distinction between dhat al-illa and wasf al-illiyya.
The intention must be concurrent with the essence of the cause, not just its activation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, one cannot use a purchased relative for kaffara; one must free a slave without such a purchase. For Zufar/Shafi'i, it is valid.
This difference affects the validity of expiation in real scenarios.

(1) أجاب عن هذا الإيراد ابن كمال باشا في «الإيضاح» (ق١/٧٥) : بأنه ذكر في الأصول أيضاً أن المعتبر مقارنة النية لذات العلة لا لوصف العلية ولذلك شرطوا الأهلية حال التعليق لا حال وجود الشرط الذي هو زمان حدوث العلية ، واللازم من منع التعليق العلية قبل وجود الشرط مقارنة النية للعلية لا مقارنتها لذات العلة.
The Book of Oaths: The Oath Regarding Emancipation and Divorce. For his expiation it is. [1] 139. 'For his expiation it is': i.e., the kaffara. This is according to us (Abu Hanifa). But according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, it does not suffice. So the conclusion is that intention must necessarily be concurrent with the cause of emancipation. They made kinship the cause of emancipation and ownership a condition, while we made it the opposite; because the Law made the purchase of a relative an emancipation. So if one purchases his father with the intention of kaffara, the intention is concurrent with the cause of emancipation. Incorrect. [1] His statement: 'for his expiation' i.e., any expiation, such as expiation of an oath, expiation of zihar, expiation of killing. [2] His statement: 'concurrent' with the cause of emancipation, on condition that the cause be voluntary, like purchasing a relative, and that the slave be a complete slave. Upon this rule are based: the non-sufficiency of emancipation from expiation in the form of the oath as previously detailed, and the author and commentator will mention it, and the non-sufficiency of emancipation of an umm walad as will come, and the non-sufficiency of emancipation of a mahram relative acquired through inheritance, for the cause there is compulsory not voluntary; as established in its place that inheritance is compulsory and cannot be dropped by dropping. [3] His statement: 'the cause of emancipation and ownership' because purchase establishes ownership, emancipation removes it, and there is contradiction between them, so purchase is not a cause for emancipation but a condition, and the cause is prohibited kinship. So if one purchases a relative with the intention of kaffara, it is not valid for it because the intention does not accompany the cause but only the condition. [4] His statement: 'contrary' according to them (Zufar and Shafi'i), purchase of a relative is the cause of emancipation, and kinship is a condition, as indicated by the hadith: 'A child cannot repay his father unless he finds him a slave and buys him and emancipates him' narrated by Muslim and the authors of the Sunan. The commentator pointed to this with his statement: 'because the Law made the purchase of a relative an emancipation.' If you say: The hadith connects emancipation with purchase using the particle 'fa' which indicates succession, even without delay, so it does not indicate that emancipation is the purchase. I say: It is like their saying: 'He gave him water and then gave him to drink' i.e., by that very act, not by another. Thus in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. (1) See: al-Minhaj (3):361, Tuhfat al-Habib (4):20, al-Mahalli (4):23, etc. (2) In Sahih Muslim (2):1148, Sunan Abi Dawud (2):757, etc. (3) al-Inaya sharh al-Hidayah (5):166. [PAGE_140] 12. Umda al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya. 'Not by purchasing a slave he swore to free' And according to them (Zufar and Shafi'i) no, where they made kinship the cause. (1) ('Not by purchasing a slave he swore to free'): i.e., he said: If I buy this slave, he is free, then he bought him with the intention of kaffara, the kaffara does not suffice because the cause of emancipation is the oath, and purchase is a condition for it, so the intention does not accompany the cause. An objection is raised against it: It was mentioned in usul al-fiqh that suspending (ta'liq) prevents causality according to us, so when the condition occurs, the suspended thing becomes a cause at that moment, so the intention would be concurrent with the cause of emancipation. [1] His statement: 'cause'; for according to them ownership is a condition, and intention is concurrent only with the condition, not with the cause, so it is not effective for sufficiency regarding kaffara. [2] His statement: 'not by purchasing'; i.e., the kaffara is not removed by purchasing a slave whose emancipation was suspended on the purchase beforehand, for the cause of emancipation in this form is the previous oath, and the condition is the purchase, and the intention only accompanied the purchase, so it is not effective for sufficiency of kaffara because the condition therein is that the intention accompany... [3] His statement: 'An objection is raised against it' etc.; the summary of the objection is that it is established in usul al-fiqh that suspending (ta'liq) prevents causality according to us, meaning that making the consequence a cause for its ruling, for example in our saying: If you enter the house you are divorced, the suspension prevents his saying 'you are divorced' from being the cause of the occurrence of divorce. Without suspension, 'you are divorced' would be a cause for it, and divorce would occur. So suspension by condition prevents it from being a cause, and when the condition occurs, the consequence becomes a cause, so his saying 'you are divorced' becomes a cause for the occurrence of divorce upon entering the house. If this is established, we say: When he suspended emancipation on purchase and said 'if I buy it then it is free', the oath was not a cause, but rather it prevented 'it is free' from being a cause for its emancipation; rather it becomes a cause only when the condition exists, which is the purchase, and the intention of kaffara is concurrent with the purchase, so it is concurrent with the cause from the perspective of its causality. Yes, if it were established that the intention must be concurrent with the essence of the cause (dhat al-illa) as well, the problem would be resolved. (1) Ibn Kamal Pasha responded to this objection in al-Idah (q1/75): that it was mentioned in usul as well that what is considered is the concurrence of intention with the essence of the cause (dhat al-illa), not with the attribute of causality (wasf al-illiyya). Therefore they required capacity (ahliyya) at the time of suspension, not at the time of the condition's existence which is the time of occurrence of causality. And the necessary consequence of the prevention of causality before the condition's existence is the concurrence of intention with causality, not its concurrence with the essence of the cause.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' is a critical refinement of a legal dispute within the Hanafi school (and also with al-Shafi'i). The core issue is the validity of emancipating a slave as expiation (kaffara) when the emancipation is accompanied by an intention (niyya) to fulfill the kaffara. The dispute centers on whether the intention must coincide with the 'cause' ('illa) of emancipation or with the 'condition' (shart). According to Abu Hanifa, the intention must be concurrent with the 'illa, not the shart. In the case of purchasing a relative, Abu Hanifa considers the 'illa to be the kinship (qaraba), while the purchase is merely a shart. Therefore, if he purchases his father with the intention of kaffara, the intention accompanies the purchase (shart), not the kinship ('illa), so the emancipation does not suffice for kaffara. Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold the opposite: the purchase is the 'illa and kinship is the shart, so intention at purchase suffices. The passage then discusses a second scenario: a person swears that if he buys a particular slave, that slave will be free. He later buys him with the intention of kaffara. Abu Hanifa says it does not suffice because the 'illa is the oath (the suspension), and the purchase is shart. The intention at purchase only accompanies the shart, not the 'illa. The passage then raises an objection based on usul al-fiqh: suspension (ta'liq) prevents the suspended thing from being a 'illa until the condition occurs. When the condition occurs, the suspended thing becomes the 'illa at that moment. So in this scenario, the 'illa (the suspended emancipation) arises at the moment of purchase, and the intention is concurrent with the purchase, so it should be valid. The response, attributed to Ibn Kamal Pasha, distinguishes between concurrence with the 'illa itself (dhat al-illa) and with its attribute of causality (wasf al-illiyya). The intention must be concurrent with the dhat al-illa, not just with the wasf al-illiyya. Since the 'illa (the suspended emancipation) existed in potency from the moment of the oath, but its causality only becomes actual at purchase, the intention at purchase is still not concurrent with the dhat al-illa, because the dhat al-illa existed before. Therefore, the emancipation is not valid for kaffara.

Key Terms
العلة (al-illa): The effective cause that brings about the ruling of emancipation.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that must exist for the ruling to apply but is not the direct cause.
ذات العلة (dhat al-illa): The essence of the cause, as opposed to its attribute of being a cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person purchases his father with the intention of fulfilling a kaffara, does the emancipation suffice?
A) Yes, because the intention was good.
B) No, because the intention accompanied the purchase (condition), not the kinship (cause).
C) Yes, because the purchase itself is the cause of emancipation.
D) No, because the father is not a valid slave.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, intention for kaffara must be concurrent with the essence of the cause (dhat al-illa), not merely with its activation (wasf al-illiyya); therefore, purchasing a relative with intention of kaffara does not suffice.

The Legal Effect of 'Lam' in Oaths: Delegable vs. Non-Delegable Actions
Explaining the legal effect of the lam in oaths of sale and similar actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting the preposition 'lam' in oaths to determine whether the oath is broken (hanth) based on the nature of the action (delegable or not).

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'lam' indicates ownership of an object if attached to a non-delegable action or object; otherwise it indicates exclusivity.

This lesson examines a crucial rule in Hanafi oath jurisprudence: how the preposition 'lam' affects the interpretation of an oath. The source passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah clarifies that the meaning of 'lam' depends on the nature of the verb—whether it admits agency (niyabah). By the end, you will be able to analyze any oath containing 'li' and determine if it indicates ownership (milk) of an object or exclusivity (ikhtisas) of an action.

Guided Path
1
Classify the Action as Delegable or Non-Delegable
Identify whether the verb in the oath can be performed by a proxy. Non-delegable actions include eating, drinking, entering, and beating a child. Delegable actions include selling and beating a slave.
This classification determines the meaning of the lam.
2
Locate the Lam
Determine whether the lam attaches to the object (noun) or to the verb. If attached to an object or to a non-delegable verb, the lam indicates ownership of the object for the addressee.
3
Apply the Ruling for Ownership
If the lam indicates ownership (object or non-delegable action), the oath is broken (hanth) by any action on that object for the addressee, regardless of whether the addressee ordered it.
Example: 'If I eat food of yours' – eating food intended for you breaks the oath even without your command.
4
Apply the Ruling for Exclusivity
If the lam indicates exclusivity (delegable action), the oath is broken only if the action is performed by the addressee's command or with his knowledge.
Example: 'If I sell a garment for you' – the oath is broken only if you ordered the sale or knew about it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن دخل على عين أو فعل لا يقعُ عن غيره : كأكل، وشرب، ودخول، وضرب الولد، اقتضى ملكه ، فحنث في : إن بعث ثوباً لك إن باغ ثوبه بلا أمره (وإن دخل على عين أو فعل لا يقعُ عن غيره : كأكل، وشرب، ودخول، وضرب الولد، اقتضى ملكه ، فحنث في : إن بعتُ ثوباً لك إن باعَ ثوبَهُ بلا أمره) لاختصاص الفعل بزيد، وذلك إنما يكون بأمره الحالف، أو بعلم الحالـف أنه باعه لأجله، سواء كان الثوب لزيد أو لغيره. انتهى. وهذا يفيد أن خصوص الأمر غير شرط ، بل يكفي في حنثه قصده البيع لأجله، سواء كان بأمره أو لا. قال في البحر»: وهذا مما يجب حفظه ، فإنّ الظاهر كلامهم هنا يخالفه معا أنه الحكم. ١ ] قوله : على عين ؛ المراد به الذات بقرينة مقابلته مع الفعل، وخلاصة المرام : إن الفعل إمّا أن يكون مما يحتمل النيابة أو لا، وعلى كل تقدير فدخولُ اللام إما على الفعل أو على مفعوله، وهو العين. فإن دخلت على فعل يحتمل النيابة عن الغيرِ اقتضت اختصاص الفعل بالمخاطب على ما مر ذكره. وإن دخلت على فعل لا يحتمل النيابة بأن لا يجري فيه التوكيل كالأكل والشرب ونحوهما، أو دخلت على العين مطلقاً، سواءً كانت الفعل من القسم الأول أو من القسم الثاني كإن بعت ثوباً لك ، وإن أكلت طعاماً لك اقتضت ملك العين للمخاطب، سواء كان الفعلُ بأمره أو بغيره. [٤] وأما ضرب الولد نحو : إن ضربتُ لك الولد فعبدُهُ حرّ، فاقتضاء الملكِ فيه غير ممكن إلا أن يراد بالملكِ الاختصاص. (وفي : كل عرس لي ، فكذا بعد قول عرسه : نكت علي، طلقت هي ، [ ۱ ] قوله : نظير الدخول ؛ المراد بالنظير المثال ، لا معناه المتعارف. [٢) قوله : وهو الثوب ؛ فإن معنى ثوباً لك ثوباً موصوفاً بكونه لك. فإن قلت : يمكن تعلقها بالفعل على هذا التقدير أيضاً. قلت : هب ؛ ولذا لو نواه صح كما صرَّحَ به في «فتح القدير»، لكن لما كانت في هذه الصورة أقرب إلى الاسم يعني الثوب بالنسبة إلى الفعل اقتضته إضافة الاسم إلى مدخولها ؛ أي كافُ المخاطب، فإنّ القرب من أسباب الترجيح ؛ ولذا إذا توسطت تعلقت بالفعل لقربه كما مرّ، مع أنه يصح هناك أيضاً جعلها حالاً من الاسم المتأخر. كذا في «الفتح». [٣] قوله : إن أكلت لك طعاماً ؛ بتقديم اللام على الاسم ؛ فإنّ الأكل لا يحتمل النيابة، فلا يصح جعلها لملك الفعل للمخاطب، فصار تقدمها على الاسم كتأخرها عنه. And if [the lam] enters upon an object or an action that does not take place on behalf of another: such as eating, drinking, entering, and beating a child, it necessitates ownership (of the object for the addressee). So he breaks his oath if he says: 'If I sell a garment of yours' and he sells his garment without his command, because the action is exclusive to Zayd, and that is only by the order of the one taking the oath, or by the knowledge of the one taking the oath that he sold it for his sake, whether the garment belongs to Zayd or another. End. And this indicates that specific command is not a condition; rather, it suffices for his breaking the oath that he intended the sale for his sake, whether by his command or not. Al-Bahr said: 'This is something that must be memorized, for apparently their statements here contradict it together with the ruling.' [1] His statement: 'upon an object' means the entity (al-dhat) by the context of its contrast with the action. The summary of the intent: the action either admits agency or not. In every case, the lam enters either upon the action or upon its object, which is the entity. If it enters upon an action that admits agency on behalf of another, it necessitates the action's being specific to the addressee, as previously mentioned. And if it enters upon an action that does not admit agency—such that agency does not apply to it, like eating and drinking and the like—or enters upon an object absolutely, whether the action is of the first type or the second type, like 'If I sell a garment of yours' and 'If I eat food of yours', it necessitates the ownership of the object for the addressee, whether the action is by his command or otherwise. [2] His statement: 'and beating a child'. As for beating a slave, it admits agency, so it falls under the first type; hence if he swore not to beat his slave and then ordered someone else to beat him, he breaks his oath, because the benefit returns to him. This is in Al-`Inayah. [3] His statement: 'necessitates ownership' i.e., the entity being owned by the addressee, so he breaks his oath by his action whether by his command or without his command, because when the action does not admit agency, it does not transfer to another than the doer, so command and its absence are equal; thus it is determined that the lam is for the exclusivity of the entity, to protect the speech from being invalid. This is in Al-`Inayah. The chapter of oaths regarding freeing slaves and divorce. And in: 'Every spouse of mine, then such-and-such...' after her saying 'his spouse: he married another, she is divorced.' This is analogous to the entry upon the object, which is the garment. As for the analogy of its entry upon an action that does not take place on behalf of another, such as his saying: 'If I eat food for you' or 'If I drink drink for you', it necessitates that the food or drink be owned by the addressee, just as in his saying: 'If I eat food of yours', for even though it is related to eating in form, it is in meaning related to the food. [4] As for beating the child, such as: 'If I beat the child for you, then his slave is free', the necessitation of ownership therein is not possible unless by ownership is meant exclusivity. (And in: 'Every spouse of mine, then such-and-such...' after her saying 'his spouse: he married another, she is divorced.' [1] His statement: 'the analogy of the entry' means the example, not its customary meaning. [2] His statement: 'which is the garment'; for the meaning of 'a garment of yours' is a garment described as being yours. If you say: It is possible for the lam to attach to the action on this assumption as well. I say: It is so; hence if he intends that, it is valid, as clarified in Fath al-Qadir. But since in this form it is closer to the noun—meaning the garment—relative to the action, it necessitates adding the noun to what it governs, i.e., the kaf of the addressee; for proximity is among the causes of preference. Hence if it intervenes, it attaches to the action due to its proximity, as previously mentioned, while it is also valid there to make it a halal from the later noun. This is in Al-Fath. [3] His statement: 'If I eat food for you' with the lam before the noun; for eating does not admit agency, so it is invalid to make it for ownership of the action for the addressee; thus its precedence over the noun is like its coming after it. [4] His statement: 'in meaning'; for the meaning on both assumptions is food described as being yours, i.e., owned. [0] His statement: 'And in: every spouse' with a kasrah on the `ayn, meaning wife, i.e., if the wife says to her husband: 'I married another woman', and the husband says: 'Every spouse of mine is divorced', divorce occurs upon every wife of his at that time, including the one who provoked it, i.e., the wife who became the cause by her oath.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on a Hanafi fiqh work explains the legal effect of the preposition 'lam' (for) in oaths. The author distinguishes between two main scenarios: when the lam enters upon a physical object (عين) or upon an action that cannot be performed by proxy (such as eating, drinking, entering, beating a child). In these cases, the lam indicates that the object is owned by the addressee, meaning the oath is broken if the person performs the action on an item belonging to the addressee, regardless of whether the addressee ordered it. Conversely, when the lam enters upon an action that can be delegated (like selling), the lam indicates that the action is to be performed for the addressee's sake; thus the oath is broken only if the action is done with the addressee's command or knowledge. The text further clarifies that beating a slave is delegable (unlike beating a child), so it falls under the first category. The same principle is applied to divorce oaths, such as when a husband says 'every spouse of mine is divorced' in response to his wife's statement about him marrying another. The commentary provides nuanced reasoning, emphasizing that the key is whether the action admits agency. The passage also addresses a potential contradiction regarding the necessity of a specific command, resolving that intention alone suffices if the action aims to benefit the addressee. This discussion is crucial for Hanafi jurists to correctly determine when an oath is broken.

Key Terms
اللام (al-lām): The preposition 'li' (for, belonging to) in oaths, indicating either ownership (ملك) or exclusivity (اختصاص) depending on the context.
النيابة (al-niyābah): Agency or delegation; whether an action can be performed by another person on behalf of the oath-taker.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership; when the lam indicates that the object is owned by the addressee, the oath is broken by acting on that object.
الاختصاص (al-ikhtiṣāṣ): Exclusivity; the lam indicates that the action or object is specific to the addressee, but not necessarily owned.
حنث (ḥanith): Breaking an oath; the consequence when the oath-taker does what he swore not to do, or fails to do what he swore to do.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'If I eat food of yours' and then eats food that is actually owned by someone else but intended for the addressee, does he break his oath according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) Yes, because the lam indicates ownership of the object for the addressee regardless of actual ownership.
B) No, because the food must be literally owned by the addressee.
C) Yes, if the addressee ordered him to eat.
D) No, because eating is a non-delegable action.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the preposition 'lam' indicates ownership of an object for the addressee when attached to a non-delegable action or an object; on a delegable action, it indicates exclusivity, requiring command or knowledge for hanth.

Manner of Flogging and Master's Authority in Hadd
Expansion of matn on hadd procedure and master's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the manner of flogging (qā'im, bila madd), calculate the slave's hadd, and explain why the master requires the imam's permission for hadd yet may enforce ta'zir independently.

Core Idea
Hadd is a divine right – only the state may enforce it; a master's authority is limited to ta'zir.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies two practical rulings and a key inter-madhhab dispute. First, how the flogging must be performed: the recipient stands and is not stretched, and the striker avoids the face and genitals. Second, a slave receives half the free person's punishment. Then it addresses whether a master may personally flog his slave for a hadd crime. The Hanafi school insists on state authorization, while the Shafi'i school grants the master full authority. We will unpack the evidence and reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
State the physical manner of flogging
Identify the requirement that the hadd recipient stands upright (qā'im) and that the striker does not stretch the person on the ground, raise the whip above his head, or drag the whip after the strike. All such actions are prohibited because they cause extra pain.
The condition 'without stretching' (bila madd) is defined in three ways, all forbidden.
2
Calculate the slave's punishment
Derive the half-punishment rule from the Quran (4:25) and the practice of Umar. The verse explicitly gives female slaves half the punishment of free women; by qiyās, male slaves also receive half. The original punishment for zina is 100 lashes, so a slave receives 50.
The passage explains the subtle reason for word order in relevant verses.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the master has complete authority (wilāyah) over his slave, just as he has for ta'zir. Therefore, he may execute hadd without the imam's permission. Some Companion practices are cited as support.
Shafi'i relies on the master's general guardianship.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes hadd (ḥaqq Allāh) from ta'zir (ḥaqq al-'abd). Hadd is a divine right that does not drop by the slave's waiver; it must be enforced by the imam or his deputy, who represent the Sharī'ah. Ta'zir is private and can be delegated.
Aḥādīth from al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī and 'Aṭā' al-Khurāsānī support assigning hadd to the sultan.
5
Apply the practical consequence (thamarāt al-khilāf)
In practice, under Hanafi law, if a master flogs his slave for zina without the imam's permission, he is liable for ta'zīr (and possibly damages). In Shafi'i law, the same act is lawful. The difference stems from the nature of the right involved.
This shows how a usul principle (nature of the right) changes practical fiqh.

[1] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي ، له أن للسيد ولاية كاملة على عبده وأمته، فتجوز له إقامة الحدودِ كما يجوز له إقامة التعزير، ومثله روي عن بعض الصحابة له أنه جلد عبده، كما في «الموطأ». ولنا : إنّ الحد حق الله ، ولذا لا يسقط بإسقاط العبد، فيستوفيه من هو نائب عن الشرع، وهو الإمام أو نائبه، بخلاف التعزير، فإنّه حق العبد. كذا في «الهداية»(4) ، ويشهد لمذهبنا قولُ الحسن البصري : «أربع إلى السلطان: الصلاة والزكاة والحدود والقصاص)، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، وعن عطاء الخراساني ه قال : «إلى السلطان : الزكاة والجمعة والحدود»(1).
(Standing in every hadd without stretching): meaning that he should not be thrown on the ground and his legs stretched. It is said: that the striker stretches his hand above his head. It is said: that he stretches the whip on the limb after striking. (And for the slave is half of it, and his master does not execute hadd on him without the permission of the imam). He said: the flogger should strike and give each limb its right, and avoid the face and the private parts. This was narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah, Abd al-Razzaq, and Sa'id ibn Mansur. In the Two Sahihs as marfu': 'When one of you strikes, let him avoid the face.' Some mashayikh also excluded the chest, but there is consideration; rather the chest is from the places of beating. Striking with a medium whip a small number does not kill on the belly, so how about the chest? Thus in al-Fath. (1) His saying: Qa'im: meaning the condition of the hadd recipient standing. Ali said: 'The man is struck standing, and the woman sitting in hadd punishments.' Narrated by Abd al-Razzaq. [2) His saying: Bila madd: meaning without stretching the flogged person on the ground, or without stretching the whip, that the striker raises it above his head, or stretches it on the body of the struck person after its fall, which increases pain. He said in al-Fath: All of this is not done. [3] His saying: Wa lil-abd: the basis for it is what passed from His statement: 'So for them is half of what is for the muhsanat from the punishment.' It was revealed about female slaves, and it includes the male slave as well, due to their sharing in slavery which diminishes [the enjoyment of] blessings and the punishment of offenses. It is confirmed from Umar (in al-Muwatta') that he flogged slaves fifty [lashes]. He only specified female slaves in the verse due to the frequency of causes of fornication among them and the prevalence of their desires. This is the subtle point in prioritizing the mention of the adulteress over the adulterer in His statement: 'The adulteress and the adulterer, flog each one of them a hundred lashes.' He prioritized the mention of the man in the verse of theft, which is His statement: 'The thief and the thiefess, cut off their hands,' because theft is based on secrecy and taking tricks, and that occurs more in men compared to women. Moreover, the witnessed evidence also testifies to the frequency of theft among men and the frequency of fornication among women. Thus in each of the two verses he prioritized what occurs more. For this subtle point he prioritized the mention of the man in the verse of stoning, because fornication after ihsan among men is more frequent compared to women. [1] His saying: Contrary to al-Shafi'i. For him, the master has complete authority over his male and female slave, so it is permissible for him to execute hadd punishments as it is permissible for him to execute ta'zir. Similarly, it is narrated from some of the Companions that he flogged his slave, as in al-Muwatta'. For us: Hadd is a right of Allah, therefore it is not dropped by the slave's waiver, so it is carried out by one who is a deputy of the Shari'ah, and that is the imam or his deputy, unlike ta'zir, for it is a right of the slave. Thus in al-Hidayah. And our madhhab is supported by the statement of al-Hasan al-Basri: 'Four matters go to the sultan: prayer, zakat, hadd, and qisas.' Narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. And from 'Ata' al-Khurasani: 'To the sultan: zakat, Jumu'ah, and hadd.'
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, explains the rulings concerning the manner of flogging in hadd punishments and the authority of the master over his slave regarding these punishments. The passage is an expansion probe: it takes the concise matn of the base text and elaborates it with proofs and clarifications.

The first ruling: the person being punished stands during flogging, without being stretched on the ground or having his legs pulled. The commentator clarifies that some define 'stretching' as raising the striker's hand above his head or extending the whip on the limb after the strike. All such practices cause extra pain and are forbidden.

The second ruling: a slave receives half the punishment of a free person. This is derived from the Quranic verse (4:25) which states that female slaves receive half the punishment of free muhsanat. By analogy, male slaves also receive half. The commentary notes that Umar ibn al-Khattab implemented this by flogging slaves fifty lashes. It also explains a subtle point: in the verse on zina (24:2), the adulteress is mentioned before the adulterer because fornication is more prevalent among women, while in the verse on theft (5:38), the thief is mentioned before the thiefess because theft is more common among men. This reasoning also applies to the priority in stoning verses.

The third ruling: a master cannot execute a hadd punishment on his own slave without the imam's permission. The Shafi'i position (opposing) holds that the master has full authority over his slave and may execute hadd just as he may execute ta'zir. The Hanafi response is that hadd is a right of Allah (haqq Allah), and thus it can only be enforced by the imam or his deputy, who represent the Shari'ah. Unlike ta'zir, which is a right of the individual (the master), hadd cannot be waived or carried out by a private individual. This is supported by statements of early authorities like al-Hasan al-Basri and 'Ata' al-Khurasani, who assign hadd to the sultan.

Overall, the passage emphasizes the public nature of hadd punishments and the requirement that they be carried out under state authority, with specific procedures to ensure justice and minimize harm.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed, divinely prescribed punishment for specific crimes (e.g., zina, theft).
مد (madd): Stretching the person being flogged on the ground, or extending the whip before or after the strike; such extra pain is prohibited.
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment, not fixed by the Shari'ah, left to the judge or master's discretion.
إمام (imam): The supreme ruler or his deputy who has authority to enforce hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require the imam's permission for a master to execute hadd on his slave?
A) Because the master lacks the physical strength to execute the punishment.
B) Because hadd is a right of Allah and must be enforced by the state authority.
C) Because the slave must give his consent before being punished.
D) Because the punishment is always half for a slave, so the imam must verify the amount.

Key Takeaway
Hadd enforcement is a state function; the master's role is limited to corrective ta'zir, and any violation imposes personal liability.

Insults Requiring Ta'zir: Definitions and Rulings
Defining insulting terms that warrant ta'zir and their legal implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify insulting expressions that warrant ta'zir in Hanafi law and explain the legal reasoning behind their punishment.

Core Idea
Verbal insults against a person's honor are sinful and subject to ta'zir, even without a fixed hadd penalty.

This passage from the chapter on ta'zir catalogs specific insulting terms and explains their definitions. Understanding ta'zir is essential for grasping how Islamic law protects personal dignity beyond the fixed hudud punishments. The Hanafi school holds that all such insults, whether direct or indirect, incur discretionary punishment to maintain public order and respect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category
Recognize that ta'zir is a discretionary punishment for sinful acts without fixed hadd or kaffarah. The passage lists insults that are subject to ta'zir.
Ta'zir is determined by the judge, not by a predefined penalty.
2
List the insulting terms and their definitions
Go through each term (e.g., laṣ, dayyuth, qurtaban) and understand its meaning as explained by the commentators. These terms are culturally loaded and their insulting nature is based on 'urf (custom).
Some terms are Persian loanwords; they are treated as insults in the Hanafi context.
3
Recognize the generality of ta'zir for insults
Note the passage's explicit statement that the ruling applies to all insulting expressions, whether in direct address ('ya laṣ') or indirect statement ('he is a thief'), and whether in second or third person.
Stylistic variation does not affect liability for ta'zir.
4
Understand the rationale for ta'zir
Ta'zir for insults serves to protect personal honor and deter public defamation. The Hanafi school views such acts as sinful (haram) even though they lack a fixed hadd punishment.
The severity of ta'zir is left to the judge's discretion, but it must be less than the harshest hadd.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بكسر الخاء المعجمة ، وكسر الزاي المعجمة ، بينهما نون ساكنة، ثمّ ياء مثناة تحتيّة ساكنة ، ثم راء مهملة – ، بالفارسية : خوك. قوله : يا تيس - بفتح التاء المثناة الفوقية، وسكون الياء المثناة التحتية، آخره سين مهملة - O thief, O pimp, O cuckold, O wine drinker, O usurer, O son of a prostitute, O son of an adulteress, you harbor thieves, you harbor adulteresses. His statement: 'O thief' — with kasra on lam and shadda on sad, also reported with fatha on lam: a thief among thieves. His statement: 'O pimp' — with fatha on dal, shadda on ya with damma, then waw sakinah, then ta marbuta: al-Zayla'i said: he who sees a man with his wife or female relative and leaves him alone with her; and it is said: he who causes two to come together for a blameworthy purpose; and it is said: he who sends his wife with a mature slave or with his sharecropper to the estate, or permits them to enter upon her in his absence. His statement: 'O cuckold' — with fatha on qaf, fatha on ta, between them a sakinah ra: Persian 'qaltaban'; it is said it is synonymous with dayyuth; and it is said: dayyuth is one who has no jealousy about someone entering upon his wife, and qurtaban is one who knows that someone is committing adultery with his wife and remains silent; and it is said: he who brings a man to his wife to obtain wealth. His statement: 'O son of a prostitute' — indicating that if he insults his lineage, he is given ta'zir for insulting the child, like 'O son of a sinner', 'O son of an infidel', and so on; and from this it is understood that if he says to a woman 'O prostitute', ta'zir is given all the more. His statement: 'you harbor thieves and you harbor adulteresses' — the change in address is merely stylistic variation; otherwise the ruling is the same for all insulting expressions, whether presented in the form of address or in the form of news, whether in the second person or third person. His statement: 'harbors thieves' — in some books 'refuge of thieves' and 'refuge of adulteresses', meaning one with whom thieves and adulterers gather and mix like friends. O one who plays with boys, O son of a forbidden one, O donkey, O pig, O dog, O he-goat, O monkey, O cupper, O son of him while his father is not so, O hirer, O catamite, O one who lowers his head, O laughingstock, O mockery. His statement: 'O one who plays with boys' — i.e., with children, and customarily applied to one who commits obscene acts with children. His statement: 'O pig' — with kasra on kha and zay, between them a sakinah nun, then ya sakinah, then ra: in Persian 'khuk'. His statement: 'O he-goat' — with fatha on ta, sukun on ya, final sin: in Persian 'buzd shti', known for frequent mounting of females; it is said in proverb 'more lustful than a he-goat'. His statement: 'O monkey' — with kasra on qaf, fatha on ra, final dal: in Persian 'buzinah'. His statement: 'and his father' — the waw is hal; meaning he said to a man 'O son of a cupper' while in fact his father is not so.

Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling from the chapter on ta'zir. It lists specific insulting expressions that incur discretionary punishment in Hanafi law. The commentary explains each term linguistically and legally. For example, 'ya laṣ' (thief) is defined as a habitual thief; 'ya dayyuth' (pimp) is defined as one who neglects his wife's honor; 'ya qurtaban' (cuckold) is a Persian loanword, sometimes synonymous with dayyuth. The passage also covers insults to lineage (e.g., 'ibn al-qahba') and collective insults (e.g., 'ta'wi al-luṣuṣ'). The overall ruling is that such insults to a person's honor are subject to ta'zir, regardless of whether they are phrased as direct address or indirect statement. The Hanafi school applies this broadly to maintain public decency and respect.

Key Terms
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment for offenses that have no fixed hadd or kaffarah, determined by the judge.
لص (laṣṣ): A thief; one who habitually steals.
ديوث (dayyūth): A man who tolerates his wife or female relative being with another man without jealousy.
قرطبان (qurṭabān): A cuckold who knows of his wife's adultery and remains silent or profits from it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No punishment as it is not a direct accusation of adultery.
B) Ta'zir, and it is even more severe than insulting a man by his mother.
C) Hadd of qadhf (false accusation of adultery).
D) No action unless she files a complaint.

Key Takeaway
All insults to a person's honor, regardless of phrasing, are sinful and subject to ta'zir in Hanafi law.

The Ruling on Singing and Chess: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Commentary on singing and chess rulings with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on singing and chess, explain the Shafi'i counter-position, and evaluate the basis for the difference.

Core Idea
Singing is not absolutely haram but becomes haram with instruments (except duff); chess is haram according to Hanafis, while Shafi'is permit it with conditions.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya tackles two issues: singing and chess. The author clarifies that singing itself is not inherently forbidden; rather, it becomes forbidden when accompanied by prohibited musical instruments, except the duff. Chess, on the other hand, is considered haram by most Hanafis, but the Shafi'i school permits it under strict conditions. This lesson unpacks the evidence and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues and their Hanafi rulings
The passage addresses singing (ghina') and chess (shatranj). The Hanafi ruling: chess is haram; singing is not absolutely haram but becomes haram when accompanied by prohibited instruments except duff.
2
Distinguish the positions on singing
Some scholars say singing is absolutely haram; others permit it absolutely. The author rejects both extremes, stating that singing is haram only when combined with forbidden instruments or content, except the duff.
3
Contrast the positions on chess
Most Hanafis rule chess as haram, while Shafi'is permit it unless it leads to gambling, delaying prayer, or foul language. The author sides with the Hanafi position.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi may not play chess or listen to singing with instruments (except duff) under any circumstances, while a Shafi'i may do so as long as it does not cause harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغناء وفي رواية أحمد وأبي يعلى والبيهقي: «مثلُ الذي يلعب بالنرد ثم يقوم يصلّي مثل الذي يتوضأ بالقيح ودم الخنزير وفي الباب أخبار أخر أيضاً بسطها الحافظ عبد العظيم المنذري في كتاب «الترغيب والترهيب»، وابن حجر المكي الهيتمي في كتابه «الزواجر عن اقتراف الكبائر». ومثله : اللعب بالشطرنج على رأي أكثر العلماء القائلين بحرمته، ومنهم أصحابنا الحنفيّة خلافاً للشافعية فإنّهم قائلون بحله إلا إذا اقترن به قمار أو إخراج صلاة عن وقتها أو سباب أو نحوها، كما فصله الدميري في حياة الحيوان عنه ذكر العقرب، وليطلب من هناك تفصيل واضع النرد والشطرنج وكيفيتهما، وسنذكر نبذاً من كيفيتهما عن قريب إن شاء الله تعالى. [١]قوله : والغناء ; - بكسر الغين المعجمة وبالمد - ، بالفارسية: سرود، وهو عطف على قوله : «لعب النرد»، وهو مشير إلى حرمة مطلق الغناء، وبه صرّح بعض مشايخنا، وقد اختلف في هذه المسألة اختلافاً فاحشاً، فمن مشدّد ومن تساهل، والحق أنه لا وجه لحرمة الغناء مطلقاً، بل هو حرام إذا اقترن به غيره من الحرمات كالآلات اللهو من الطبل والكوبة والبربط وغيرها مما يضرب عند الغناء إلا الدف. فإنّ المعازف أي المزامير كلّها حرام إلا الدف، بذلك وردت الأخبار، ولا عبرة لقول من حرم الدف أيضاً أو مطلق الغناء، وكذا لا عبرة لقول من أباح جميع المزامير كائنا من كان، فإنّه لا قولَ لأحدٍ بعد ورود الأحاديث الصحيحة الدالة على حرمة جميع المزامير إلا الدف، وللتفصيل موضع آخر.
And singing. And in the narration of Ahmad, Abu Ya'la, and al-Bayhaqi: 'The example of the one who plays backgammon and then stands to pray is like the one who performs wudu with pus and pig's blood.' And in this chapter there are other traditions as well, which al-Hafiz 'Abd al-'Azim al-Mundhiri expanded in his book 'al-Targhib wa al-Tarhib', and Ibn Hajar al-Makki al-Haytami in his book 'al-Zawajir 'an Iqtiraf al-Kaba'ir'. And similar to it is playing chess according to the opinion of most scholars who deem it prohibited, among them our Hanafi companions, contrary to the Shafi'is, for they deem it permissible unless accompanied by gambling, delaying prayer beyond its time, cursing, or the like, as al-Damiri detailed in 'Hayat al-Hayawan' under the mention of the scorpion. Let the details of the inventor of backgammon and chess and their methods be sought from there, and we will mention a sample of their methods shortly, God willing. [1] His statement: 'and singing' - with a kasra on the 'ghayn' and with madd, in Persian: 'surud', and it is conjuncted to his statement 'playing backgammon', and it indicates the prohibition of absolute singing, and some of our teachers explicitly stated this. And there has been excessive disagreement on this issue, some severe and some lenient. The truth is that there is no basis for the absolute prohibition of singing; rather, it is forbidden if accompanied by other prohibitions such as instruments of amusement like the drum, the kubah, the barbat, and others that are played during singing, except the duff. For all musical instruments, i.e., wind instruments, are forbidden except the duff; traditions have come to that effect, and no attention is paid to the statement of one who forbids the duff as well or absolute singing. Likewise, no attention is paid to the statement of one who permits all wind instruments, whoever he may be, for no one's opinion is valid after the appearance of authentic traditions indicating the prohibition of all wind instruments except the duff. For details, there is another place.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of singing and chess within the Hanafi school. The author begins by quoting a hadith that compares one who plays backgammon and then prays to one who performs wudu with impure substances, indicating the severity of such games. He then states that most scholars, including the Hanafis, deem chess haram. However, the Shafi'is permit it unless combined with gambling, delaying prayer, or foul language. The author then focuses on singing ('ghina'), clarifying that it is not absolutely haram. Rather, it becomes haram when accompanied by forbidden instruments like drums, except the duff (a type of tambourine). All wind instruments (mazamir) are haram except the duff, based on authentic hadith. The commentary refutes those who either absolutely prohibit singing or permit all instruments, emphasizing that the only reliable position is the prohibition of all instruments except the duff. This is a typical Hanafi position that distinguishes between the act of singing itself and the means accompanying it.

Key Terms
الغناء (al-ghina'): Singing, specifically vocal music with or without instruments. The author argues it is not absolutely haram but becomes haram with prohibited instruments except the duff.
الشطرنج (al-shatranj): Chess, considered by most Hanafis as haram due to resemblance to games of chance and waste of time.
المعازف (al-ma'azif): Musical instruments, especially wind instruments (mazamir), all of which are haram except the duff (frame drum).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Singing is permissible with any instrument as long as the lyrics are religious.

Key Takeaway
Singing is not inherently haram; it becomes haram with prohibited instruments (except duff). Chess is haram for Hanafis, but Shafi'is permit it under conditions.

Prohibition of Musical Instruments Except the Duff
A ruling on the prohibition of musical instruments and singing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wind instruments and the duff exception, and identify the proof from authentic ahadith.

Core Idea
All wind instruments are haram except the daff, established by authentic ahadith.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text clarifies the prohibition of musical instruments, distinguishing between wind instruments (mazamir) which are haram and the handheld drum (daff) which is permitted. It also dismisses extreme positions, grounding the ruling in authentic prophetic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what specific act is being ruled upon and its legal classification (haram, halal). Here, playing wind instruments (mazamir) is haram, while the daff is excepted.
Note that singing is not intrinsically haram; it becomes haram only when accompanied by prohibited instruments.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the evidence cited. The author refers to authentic ahadith (sunnah) that indicate the prohibition of all mazamir except the daff.
These ahadith are ahad (single-transmitter), thus zanni al-thubut, but their indication (dalala) is qati' according to the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the dalil proves the ruling. The explicit mention of 'ma'azif' in the hadith is interpreted as a general prohibition that is restricted to wind instruments by the exception of the daff.
The Hanafi position uses the principle that a general command/statement indicates obligation/prohibition until specified.
4
Dismiss extreme views
Recognize that the author dismisses two extreme opinions: those who prohibit the daff or all singing, and those who permit all wind instruments. This guards against both extremes.
These dismissals are based on the strength of the authentic ahadith.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is that all wind instruments (e.g., flute, trumpet) are haram, while the duff is halal in appropriate contexts like weddings and Eid.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لحرمة الغناء مطلقاً، بل هو حرام إذا اقترن به غيره من الحرمات كالآلات اللهو من الطبل والكوبة والبربط وغيرها مما يضرب عند الغناء إلا الدف. فإنّ المعازف أي المزامير كلّها حرام إلا الدف، بذلك وردت الأخبار، ولا عبرة لقول من حرم الدف أيضاً أو مطلق الغناء، وكذا لا عبرة لقول من أباح جميع المزامير كائنا من كان، فإنّه لا قولَ لأحدٍ بعد ورود الأحاديث الصحيحة الدالة على حرمة جميع المزامير إلا الدف، وللتفصيل موضع آخر.
For the prohibition of singing absolutely, rather it is haram when accompanied by other prohibitions such as instruments of amusement like the drum, kubah, barbat, and others that are struck at the time of singing except the daff. Indeed, musical instruments, i.e., wind instruments, are all haram except the daff. The reports have come with that. And no consideration is given to the saying of one who prohibits the daff as well or singing absolutely. Likewise, no consideration is given to the saying of one who permits all wind instruments, whoever he may be. For there is no saying for anyone after the authentic ahadith have come indicating the prohibition of all wind instruments except the daff. And for detail, there is another place.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the prohibition of musical instruments and singing. It begins by stating that singing is not absolutely haram; rather it becomes haram when accompanied by other prohibitions, such as musical instruments like the drum (tabl), kubah, and barbat, except the daff (a handheld drum without jingles). Then it clarifies that all mazamir (wind instruments) are haram except the daff, and that authentic ahadith support this. The author then dismisses two extreme views: those who prohibit the daff itself or all singing, and those who permit all wind instruments. He asserts that no opinion can stand against the authentic ahadith. The Hanafi position is clearly that all musical instruments except the daff are haram. The daff is permitted in weddings and other occasions as per authentic reports. This passage guards against both excessive strictness and excessive leniency regarding musical instruments.

Key Terms
المعازف (al-ma'azif): Musical instruments generally; here specifically wind instruments (mazamir) are meant.
المزامير (al-mazamir): Wind instruments; all types are ruled haram except the daff.
الدف (al-duff): A handheld drum without jingles; permitted in specific contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All musical instruments are haram, including the daff.
B) Wind instruments (mazamir) are haram, but the daff is permitted.
C) All wind instruments are halal except the daff.
D) Singing without instruments is haram in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules all wind instruments (mazamir) as haram, based on authentic ahadith, with the sole exception of the daff.

Definition and Classification of Theft in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines theft and classifies it, with a structural query

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of theft, distinguish it from pickpocketing (ṭarr), and classify it into small and grand theft.

Core Idea
Theft in Hanafi law is defined by five precise conditions; its dual nature (hadd + daman) warrants a separate book.

This passage introduces the Book of Theft by providing its linguistic and legal definitions, clarifying what distinguishes theft from other forms of taking property, such as pickpocketing. It also explains why theft is treated in its own book rather than within the Book of Hadd, and it classifies theft into two types: small theft (sariqa ṣughrā) and grand theft (sariqa kubrā, i.e., highway robbery). Understanding this classification is essential for applying the correct penalties and legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
Find the linguistic meaning (taking secretly) and the legal definition (taking valuable property from a secure place belonging to a non-related person without ambiguity, secretly, while the owner intends to protect it during sleep or absence). Note each condition.
The legal definition contains five key elements: valuable property, secure place, non-related person, no ambiguity, secrecy during owner's inattentiveness.
2
Distinguish theft from pickpocketing
The author introduces ṭarr (pickpocketing) as taking property from a person who is present, awake, and intending to protect it. This is not considered sariqa in Hanafi law because the element of concealment from the owner's awareness is missing.
Pickpocketing may be subject to ta'zir rather than hadd.
3
Understand the structural objection and answer
The passage raises a question: why is theft a separate book instead of a chapter in the Book of Hadd? The answer is that theft also involves civil liability (ḍamān), so it merits independent treatment.
This reflects the Hanafi approach of organizing fiqh by topics that combine multiple legal dimensions.
4
Classify the two types of theft
Small theft (sariqa ṣughrā) is ordinary theft from the owner or his representative (e.g., depositary, borrower). Grand theft (sariqa kubrā) is highway robbery where property is taken from the protection of the Imam (state authority). Both require secrecy, but the victim differs.
Grand theft often carries more severe penalties, including death in certain cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة قوله : كتاب السرقة ؛ هو لغة : بمعنى أخذ الشيء من الغير خفية، وشرعاً : أخذ مال معتبر من حرز أجنبي لا شبهة فيه خفية، وهو قاصد للحفظ في نومه أو غيبته، وأخذ الشيء من الغير، وهو حاضر يقظان قاصد حفظه يسمى طراً، وفاعله يقال : الطرار، كذا في «كليات أبي البقاء» وغيره. وذكر في «القاموس» وغيره : إنّ السّرقة مصدر لسَرَقَ يَسرق ، من باب ضَرَب، - وهو بفتح السين المهملة، وكسر الراء المهملة، وجاءَ بضم السين، وسكون الراء - ، وجاء أيضا سَرَق : - بفتح السين، وفتح الراء، وبفتح السين وكسر الراء، وبفتح السين وسكون الراء - . والسرقة : - بفتح السين وسكون الراء، ويضم السين مع سكون الراء، ويفتح السين مع كسر الراء - : اسم، وقد جرت عادة الفقهاء بذكر بحث السرقة بعنوان الكتاب. واعترض عليه : بأن حد السرقة كغيره من الحدود، فلم لم يدرجوه في كتاب الحدود؟ ولم لم يعنونوه بالباب، كما فعلوا في حد الزنى وغيره. وأجيب عنه : بأن بحث السّرقة لما كان متضمناً للضمان أيضاً أفردوا له كتاباً، ثمّ السرقة على نوعين : ١ . سرقة صغرى ؛ وهي المتبادرة عند إطلاق السرقة. ٢. وسرقة كبرى ؛ وهي المسماة بقطع الطريق، ويعتبر في كل منهما أخذ المال خفية، لكن في الصغرى عن عين المالك أو من يقوم مقامه كالمودع والمستعير، وفي الكبرى عن عين الإمام الملتزم حفظ حقوق المسلمين وبلادهم كذا في «الفتح» (۲۷۹) (۲) «فتح القدير»(٥ : ٣٩٠).
Book of Theft. He said: The Book of Theft; linguistically it means taking something from another secretly. Legally: taking valuable property from a secure place belonging to a non-related person without ambiguity, secretly, while he intends to protect it during his sleep or absence. Taking something from another while he is present, awake, intending to protect it is called tarar (pickpocketing) and its doer is called tarrar. This is from Kulliyat Abi al-Baqa and others. Mentioned in al-Qamus and others that al-sarq is a verbal noun from saraqa yasriq, from the form daraba, with fatha on sin and kasra on ra', and also with damm on sin and sukun on ra', and also saraq with fatha on both sin and ra', with fatha on sin and kasra on ra', with fatha on sin and sukun on ra'. Al-sarqa (with fatha on sin and sukun on ra', or damm on sin and sukun on ra', or fatha on sin and kasra on ra') is the noun. The jurists are accustomed to discussing theft under the title 'Kitab'. It was objected: The hadd of theft is like other hadd punishments, so why did they not include it in the Book of Hadd? And why did they not title it as a chapter (Bab), as they did for the hadd of zina and others? The answer: Because the discussion of theft also includes guarantee (daman), they gave it a separate book. Then theft is of two types: 1. Small theft, which is what is understood when the word theft is used absolutely. 2. Grand theft, which is called highway robbery. In each, taking property secretly is required, but in small theft, it must be from the owner himself or his representative like the depositary or borrower; in grand theft, it must be from the Imam who is responsible for protecting the rights and lands of Muslims. As in al-Fath (279), Fath al-Qadir (5:390).
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Theft. It begins with linguistic and legal definitions. Legally, theft requires: (1) taking valuable property (مال معتبر), (2) from a secure place (حرز), (3) belonging to a non-related person (أجنبي), (4) without ambiguity of ownership (لا شبهة فيه), and (5) secretly (خفية). The offender must intend to protect the property during the owner's sleep or absence. Pickpocketing (الطر) is distinguished: taking from a person who is awake and protecting the property. The passage then addresses a structural question: why treat theft as a separate book (كتاب) rather than a chapter (باب) in the Book of Hadd? The answer is that theft includes both the fixed punishment (حد) and civil liability (ضمان), so it warrants its own book. Finally, theft is classified into two types: small theft (سرقة صغرى) which is ordinary theft from the owner or his representative, and grand theft (سرقة كبرى) which is highway robbery (قطع الطريق), where the property is taken from the protection of the Imam (the state). The classification is important for applying different rulings.

Key Terms
السرقة (al-sariqa): Legally: taking valuable property from a secure place belonging to a non-related person without ambiguity, secretly, while the owner intends to protect it during sleep or absence.
الطر (al-ṭarr): Taking property from a person who is present and awake and intending to protect it; pickpocketing.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Legal liability for property, especially the obligation to compensate for stolen goods.
سرقة صغرى (sariqa ṣughrā): Small theft: taking property from the owner or his representative (e.g., depositary, borrower) secretly.
سرقة كبرى (sariqa kubrā): Grand theft: highway robbery (قطع الطريق), taking property from the protection of the Imam (state authority).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because theft is more common than other hadd offenses
B) Because theft involves both a fixed punishment (hadd) and civil liability (daman)
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered it to be a separate book
D) Because theft includes both small and large types

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi legal definition of theft is precise, requiring a valuable item taken secretly from a secure place without ambiguity while the owner is absent or asleep; theft is further classified into small and grand types.

Hiraz and the Confession Requirement: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Defines hiraz and explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on confession count.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two types of hiraz according to the Hanafi school, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the number of confessions required for the hadd of theft, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Hiraz is either by place or guardian; one confession suffices for theft, unlike zina's exceptional requirement.

This passage from Al-Hidayah's commentary defines 'hiraz' (secure place) and then addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: Must a thief confess once or twice for the hadd to be applied? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold one confession sufficient, while Abu Yusuf initially required two by analogy to zina. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school manages textual exceptions and analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define hiraz and its two types
Identify the two categories: intrinsic hiraz (e.g., house, chest) and extrinsic hiraz (e.g., mosque with a guardian).
Intrinsic: enclosed and permission required; extrinsic: guarded but not enclosed.
2
Identify the ruling
Note the majority view: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad – one confession suffices.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf requires two confessions, by qiyas to zina (each confession like one witness).
He initially held this view, later retracted.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response: Zina's requirement of four confessions is text-based and contrary to qiyas; theft remains on the general principle of one confession.
The principle: al-mar'u mu'akhadhab bi iqrarihi (a person is held by his confession).
5
Determine the tarjih
Abu Yusuf retracted; the mufta bihi is one confession.
This is confirmed in Al-Binaya and other sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمكان كبيت، أو صندوق، أو بحافظ كجالس في طريق أو مسجد . عنده ماله ، وأقر بها مرة هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد له، وعند أبي يوسف له لا بد أن يقر مرتين قياساً على الزنا ، فإنَّ كلَّ إقرار بمثابة شاهد واحد. قلنا: إنما يشترط الأربعةُ في الزِّنا بالنَّصَّ على خلاف القياس، وفيما سواه بقي على الأصل، وهو أن المرء مؤاخذ بإقراره. قوله : بمكان ؛ تتعلّق بقوله : «محرزاً»، قال في «البحر» : الحرز على قسمين : ۱. حرز بنفسه : وهو كل بقعة معدة للإحراز ممنوع عن الدخول فيها إلا بإذن كالدور والحوانيت والخيم والخزائن والصناديق. ٢ .أو بغيره : وهو كل مكان غير معد للإحراز، فيه حافظ : كالمساجد والطرق والصحراء. قوله : مرة ؛ متعلق بقوله : «أقر» ؛ وحاصله : إن الإقرار بالسرقة يكفي لإقامة الحد. قوله : هذا ؛ إشارة إلى قوله : «وأقر بها مرة». قوله : وعند أبي يوسف له ؛ هذا قوله الأول، ويروي عنه له أنه يشترط كون الإقرارين في مجلسين مختلفين، وذكر بشر رجوع أبي يوسف له إلى قولهما. كذا في «البناية». قوله : قياساً على الزنا ؛ فإنه يشترط فيه الإقرار أربع مرات كما تشترط أربعة شهود. قوله : : قلنا ... الخ ؛ جواب عن قول أبي يوسف له من قبلهما، وحاصله : إن مقتضى القياس في الزنا أيضاً أن يعتبر الإقرار مرّة واحدة، فاشتراط الأربع فيه بالنص الوارد فيه على خلاف القياس فلا يقاس عليه غيره.
In a place like a house, a chest, or due to a guardian like a person sitting in a road or mosque having his property with him, and he confessed to it once. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, whereas according to Abu Yusuf, he must confess twice, by analogy to zina, for every confession is like one witness. We said: The requirement of four in zina is by text contrary to analogy, and in other matters the principle remains, which is that a person is held accountable for his confession. "His saying: 'in a place' relates to his saying 'secured'. Al-Bahr says: Hiraz is of two types: 1. Hiraz by itself: every place prepared for securing, forbidden to enter except by permission, like houses, shops, tents, vaults, and chests. 2. Or by others: every place not prepared for security, but has a guard, like mosques, roads, and desert. "His saying: 'once' relates to his saying 'he confessed'; the gist is that a confession to theft suffices to establish the hadd. "His saying: 'this' refers to his saying 'and he confessed to it once.' "His saying: 'according to Abu Yusuf' is his first opinion, and it is narrated from him that he conditions the two confessions be in two different sessions, and Bashr mentioned that Abu Yusuf retracted to their opinion. Thus in Al-Binaya. "His saying: 'by analogy to zina' – for it requires confession four times just as four witnesses are required. "His saying: 'We said...' is the response to Abu Yusuf's statement from their side. The gist: The requirement of the analogy in zina also considers confession once, so requiring four there is by text contrary to analogy, so nothing else is analogized to it.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage first defines 'hiraz' in two categories: intrinsic hiraz (places like houses) and extrinsic hiraz (places like mosques with a guardian). It then addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: how many confessions are required to establish the hadd of theft? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say one confession is sufficient, based on the general principle that a person is accountable for his own confession. Abu Yusuf argues for two confessions, drawing an analogy to zina, where four confessions are needed, essentially equating each confession to one witness. The text responds by rebutting that zina's requirement is exceptional due to explicit text contrary to analogy, so it cannot be analogized to theft. The commentary notes that Abu Yusuf is reported to have eventually retracted and agreed with the majority view.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession of guilt by the thief; one confession is sufficient for hadd.
حرز (hiraz): A secured place: either physically enclosed (e.g., house) or with a guardian (e.g., guarded market).
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used by Abu Yusuf to require two confessions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position on the number of confessions required for the hadd of theft?
A) Four confessions
B) Two confessions in different sessions
C) One confession
D) No confession needed if there is a witness

Key Takeaway
Hiraz has two forms (intrinsic and extrinsic), and one confession suffices for theft; the Hanafi school rejects analogy to zina because zina's requirement is textually exceptional.
