Khums Eligibility: Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib
Explains Prophet's lineage to distinguish Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib for khums eligibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the lineage of the Prophet (PBUH), distinguish Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib from other Quraysh, and state the Hanafi ruling that khums is restricted to these two clans.

Core Idea
Khums is reserved for Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib — only these two clans from the Prophet's lineage.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah may seem purely biographical, but it serves a critical legal function: defining exactly who qualifies for the khums (fifth) share of spoils. In Hanafi fiqh, khums is not distributed to all Quraysh; it is limited to the clans of Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib. To apply this rule correctly, one must know the Prophet's genealogy and where these two clans begin and end. This lesson extracts the legal implications from the genealogical details.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Rule
Read the opening sentence: 'it has been established that it (khums) was given only to Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib.' Note that the source does not discuss the permissibility of khums itself but confirms who is entitled.
This rule is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.
2
Trace the Lineage Nodes
Follow the lineage from the Prophet up to Adnan: Muhammad → Abdullah → Abdul Muttalib → Hashim → Abd Manaf → Qusayy → Kilab → Murra → Ka'b → Lu'ayy → Ghalib → Fihr (Quraysh) → Malik → al-Nadr → Kinana → Khuzayma → Mudrika → Ilyas → Mudar → Nizar → Ma'add → Adnan.
The key ancestors for the clans are Hashim and al-Muttalib (brothers).
3
Define Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib
Bani Hashim descend from Hashim ibn Abd Manaf; Bani Muttalib descend from al-Muttalib ibn Abd Manaf, Hashim's brother. The passage confirms these two are the exclusive recipients.
Other descendants of Abd Manaf (e.g., Bani Abd Shams) are excluded.
4
Apply the Rule to Khums Distribution
Conclude that when distributing spoils (ghanimah), the khums portion must be given only to members of these two clans, not to all Quraysh or other relatives.
This is the practical fiqh result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو محمدُ بنُ عبدِ الله بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف النبي ، وقد ثبت أنه لم يعط إلا لبني هاشم وبني المطلب، فسره الشارح بقوله ؛ أي لبني هاشم وبني المطلب، ثمّ فصَّلَ الكلام في نسبه الشريف، وما يتعلّق به، بحيث يعلم به الفرق بين بني هاشم وبني المطلب، وبين غيرهم.
[١]قوله : هو محمد ؛ هو أشهر أسمائه ، وهو الذي علمه ملك في منام والدته آمنة بنت وهب الزهري ، فقال : إذا وضعت شمس الفلاح والهدى فسمّيه محمد، وإنما سُمّي به لكونه ممن يحمده الخلائق الأوّلون والآخرون في الدنيا والآخرة.
ومن أسمائه الله المشهورة في الأزمنة السابقة أحمد ، وهو الذي بشر الله به نبيه موسى ، كما ورد في الحديث، وبشر به نبيه عيسى عليه السلام كما حكاه الله في سورة الصف من القرآن، وهو اسم لم يسم به أحدٌ قبله من لدن آدم إلى عصره، لم يسم أحد بيحيى من عهدِ آدم إلى عهد يحيى بن زكريا على نبينا وعليهم الصلاة والسلام. وأما محمّد فقد سُمّي به جمع من العرب أبنائهم لما سمعوا أنه سيبعث نبي في العرب اسمه محمّد رجاء أن يتفضّل الله عليهم بهذه الفضيلة، وله سوى هذين أسماء كثيرة سردَ منها مؤلّف دلائل الخيرات مئتين وواحداً، هذا خلاصة ما ذكره أحمد بن حجر المكي الهيتمي في رسالته في المولد النبي المسماة بـ«النعمة الكبرى على العالم»، وفي شرح القصيدة الهمزية المسمى بـ«المنح المكية».
[١] قوله : ابن عبد الله ؛ كنيته أبو قثم ، أو أبو محمد، أو أبو أحمد، ولقب بالذبيح، وكانت وفاته حين كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في بطن أمه، وأبوه عبد المطلب - بضم الميم، وتشديد الطاء المهملة وكسر اللام - ، اسمه عامر أو شيبة الحمد، والثاني هو الصحيح، وإنما سمّي به لأنه ولد وفي رأسه شيبة، وهو أول من خضب بالسوادِ من العرب، وكنيته أبو الحارث.
وإنما اشتهر بعبد المطلب ؛ لأن أباه هاشماً قال لأخيه المطلب بن عبد مناف حين حضرته الوفاة، وهو بمكة أدرك : عبدك بيثرب، وكان عبد المطلب عند ذلك بالمدينة الطيبة المسمّاة في الجاهليّة بيثرب - بفتح الياء المثناة التحتية ، وسكون الثاء المثلثة ، وفتح الراء المهملة ، وقيل : بكسرها ، آخره باء موحدة – .
وقيل : إنّ عمّه المطلب جاءَ به إلى مكة وهو بهيئة بذة ، فكان يسأل عنه الناس، فيقول المطلب: هو عبدي حياءً من أن يقول : ابن أخي، وهو بتلك الهيئة الرديئة، فلما أحسن حاله أظهر أنه ابن أخيه.
:
وولد لعبد المطلب الغيداق، وقتم، وعبد الكعبة، والحارث، والزبير، وحجل، وضرار، والمقوّم، وأبو لهب، والعبّاس وحمزة، وأبو طالب، وعبد الله، وقيل : أولاده عشرة ، وقيل : اثنا عشرة، وهو أصغرهم على المشهور، لكنَّ الصحيح خلافه، فإنّ حمزة أصغر منه ، والعباس أصغر من حمزة.
وكان عمر عبد المطلب مئة وأربعين سنة ، وقيل : مئة وعشرين، وكانت وفاته حين كان عمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ثمان سنين أو ست أو تسع أو عشر على اختلاف الروايات ، والأول هو المشهور.
وأما والد عبد المطلب هاشم فاسمه عمر، وإنما لقب بهاشم ؛ لأنه كان يهشم الثريد لقومه في الجدب والقحط.
ووالده عبد مناف - بفتح الميم – واسمه المغيرة، وكانت والدته أخدمته صنماً عظيماً مسمّى بمناة، فقيل له : عبد مناة، ورأى أبوه يوافق اسمه اسم عبد مناة ابن كنانة، فغيره إلى عبد مناف. وهو ابن قصي مصغراً واسمه زيداً ويزيد أو مجمع، ولما كانت أمه فاطمة بنت سعد احتملته في بلاد قضاعة فبعد عن عشيرته ودخل مكة بعد مدة سُمّى قصيّاً، وقصي هو ابن كلاب - بكسر الكاف ابن مُرّة بضم الميم وتشديد الراء المهملة – ابن كعب بن لؤي - مصغرا - ابن غالب بن فهر – بكسر الفاء ، واسمه قريش.
-
وإليه ينسب القرشي ابن مالك بن النضر بن كنانة - بالكسر – ابن خزيمة بن مدركة بن إلياس بن مضر بن نزار - بالكسر - ابن مَعَد - بفتحتين وتشديد الدال المهملة - ابن عدنان – بالفتح - .
إلى هاهنا تحقق النسب الشريف، وما فوقه وقع فيه اختلاف كثير بعد الاتفاق على أنّ عدنان من ولد إسماعيل ذبيح الله ابن ابراهيم خليل الله صلى الله على نبينا
Know that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is Muhammad son of Abdullah son of Abdul Muttalib son of Hashim son of Abd Manaf the Prophet, and it has been established that it (khums) was given only to Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib. The commentator explained it by his saying: 'i.e., to Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib.' Then he detailed the speech about his noble lineage and what pertains to it, such that the distinction between Bani Hashim, Bani Muttalib, and others is known by it. [1] His saying: 'He is Muhammad'; it is his most famous name, and it was taught by an angel in the dream of his mother Amina bint Wahb al-Zuhri, who said: 'When you place the sun of success and guidance, name him Muhammad.' He was named thus because he is one whom the first and last creatures praise in this world and the Hereafter. Among His well-known names in previous times is Ahmad, with which Allah gave glad tidings to His prophet Moses, as narrated in the hadith, and gave glad tidings to His prophet Jesus, peace be upon him, as Allah related in Surah al-Saff of the Qur'an. It is a name no one before him was named from Adam to his time, just as no one was named Yahya from Adam's time to the time of Yahya ibn Zakariyya. As for Muhammad, a group of Arabs named their children thus when they heard that a prophet would be sent among the Arabs named Muhammad, hoping that Allah would favor them with this virtue. Besides these two, he has many names, two hundred and one of which were listed by the author of Dala'il al-Khayrat. This is the summary of what Ahmad ibn Hajar al-Makki al-Haytami mentioned in his treatise on the Prophet's birth called 'al-Ni'mat al-Kubra 'ala al-'Alam' and in the commentary on the Hamziyya poem called 'al-Minah al-Makkiyya.' [1] His saying: 'son of Abdullah'; his patronymic is Abu Qutham, or Abu Muhammad, or Abu Ahmad, and he was nicknamed al-Dhabih (the sacrificed). He died while the Prophet was in his mother's womb. His father Abdul Muttalib — with damma on the mim, tashdid on the ta and kasra on the lam — his name is 'Amir or Shaybat al-Hamd, the second being correct. He was so named because he was born with a white streak in his hair (shayba). He was the first to dye with black among the Arabs. His patronymic is Abu al-Harith. He became famous as 'Abd al-Muttalib because his father Hashim said to his brother al-Muttalib ibn Abd Manaf at the time of his death while in Mecca: 'Your servant is in Yathrib.' At that time, Abdul Muttalib was in the blessed city of Medina, called in the pre-Islamic era Yathrib — with fatha on the ya, sukun on the tha, fatha on the ra — and some say with kasra on the ra, its final letter being ba. It is said that his uncle al-Muttalib brought him to Mecca in a shabby state, and he would ask people about him, saying: 'He is my servant,' out of shyness to say he was his nephew in that poor condition. When his situation improved, he revealed that he was his son. Abdul Muttalib had sons: al-Ghaydaq, Qutham, Abd al-Ka'ba, al-Harith, al-Zubayr, Hujl, Dirar, al-Muqawwim, Abu Lahab, al-Abbas, Hamza, Abu Talib, and Abdullah. It is said his sons were ten, and it is said twelve. He was the youngest according to the famous view, but the correct view is contrary, for Hamza was younger than him, and al-Abbas younger than Hamza. Abdul Muttalib's age was one hundred and forty years, and it is said one hundred and twenty. He died when the Prophet was eight years old, or six, or nine, or ten according to different narrations; the first is the famous one. As for Hashim, the father of Abdul Muttalib, his name is 'Amr. He was nicknamed Hashim because he used to crumble bread (hashima al-tharid) for his people in drought and famine. His father Abd Manaf — with fatha on the mim — his name is al-Mughira. His mother had him serve a great idol called Manat, so he was called Abd Manat. His father saw that his name coincided with the name of Abd Manat ibn Kinana, so he changed it to Abd Manaf. He is the son of Qusayy, diminutive, his name being Zayd, Yazid, or Mujammi'. When his mother Fatima bint Sa'd took him to the land of Quda'a, he became distant from his tribe and entered Mecca after a while, thus called Qusayy. Qusayy is the son of Kilab — with kasra on the kaf — ibn Murra — with damma on the mim and tashdid on the ra — ibn Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy — diminutive — ibn Ghalib ibn Fihr — with kasra on the fa', his name being Quraysh. To him the Qurayshi is attributed. Then Ibn Malik ibn al-Nadr ibn Kinana — with kasra — ibn Khuzayma ibn Mudrika ibn Ilyas ibn Mudar ibn Nizar — with kasra — ibn Ma'add — with two fatha and tashdid on the dal — ibn Adnan — with fatha. To this point the noble lineage is verified; what is above it has much disagreement, after agreement that Adnan is from the descendants of Isma'il, the sacrificed one of Allah, son of Ibrahim, the friend of Allah. May Allah bless our Prophet.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biographical exposition of the lineage of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), provided by the commentator (sharh) of Al-Wiqayah. Its purpose is to define who are the Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib, as these two clans are the exclusive recipients of the khums (fifth) share from booty, according to established Sunnah. The author begins by affirming the Prophet's name and lineage, then traces each ancestor up to Adnan. The commentary explains the significance of the name 'Muhammad' and 'Ahmad', and clarifies the etymology and history of names like 'Hashim', 'Abd Manaf', and 'Qusayy'. It also lists the sons of Abdul Muttalib, including the Prophet's father Abdullah, and notes the ages and deaths of forefathers. The text emphasizes that the khums share is not given to other Quraysh clans, only to these two. This lesson is primarily biographical but serves a legal function: to make the distinction clear for the application of the rule on distribution of spoils. The commentator does not engage in legal reasoning here but provides the genealogical information necessary for the ruling.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Bani Hashim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) descending from Hashim ibn Abd Manaf, including the Prophet and his relatives.
بني المطلب (Bani Muttalib): The clan descending from al-Muttalib ibn Abd Manaf, considered alongside Bani Hashim for khums eligibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which groups are entitled to khums from spoils?
A) All descendants of Quraysh
B) Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib only
C) Bani Hashim only
D) The Prophet's immediate family (Ahl al-Bayt) only

Key Takeaway
Khums is exclusively for Bani Hashim and Bani Muttalib; knowing the Prophet's genealogy is essential to apply this Hanafi ruling correctly.

The Recipients of Khums Dhawi al-Qurba: Evidence from the Prophet's Practice
Hadith proof for confining khums Dhawi al-Qurba to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the recipients of the khums share of Dhawi al-Qurba and identify the hadith evidence that limits it to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.

Core Idea
Prophet's khums share for relatives is exclusively for Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.

This lesson examines a key hadith used by the Hanafi school to determine the recipients of the khums share for Dhawi al-Qurba (close relatives of the Prophet). The passage reports the Prophet's distribution after Khaybar and his response to the objection of Uthman and Jubayr. Understanding this evidence is essential for grasping the Hanafi position that this share is limited to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, excluding other Quraysh clans.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its proof
The ruling: the khums share for Dhawi al-Qurba is wajib and given to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib. The proof is the hadith from Jubayr ibn Mut'im.
The passage cites multiple narrations of the same hadith, indicating its authenticity.
2
Analyze the hadith's content
The hadith shows the Prophet's action and words: he gave the share only to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, and when questioned, he declared them 'one thing'.
The Prophet's statement is a decisive legal indication (dalalah qati) despite the hadith being ahad.
3
Understand the objection and response
Uthman (from Banu Abd Shams) and Jubayr (from Banu Nawfal) objected, but the Prophet justified exclusion because Banu al-Muttalib supported him in both Jahiliyyah and Islam.
The reason given is specific to Banu al-Muttalib, not a general criterion for all relatives.
4
Contrast with Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school interprets 'Dhawi al-Qurba' broadly to include all Quraysh. The Hanafi position relies on this specific practice to restrict the general Quranic verse.
This difference is a practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) affecting distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] قوله : ولما قسم....الخ ; أخرج أبو داود والنسائي عن جبير بن مطعم له قال : «لَمّا قسم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سهم ذوي القربى من خيبر بين بني هاشم وبني المطلب جئت أنا وعثمان فقلنا : يا رسولُ الله هؤلاء بنو هاشم لا ننكر فضلهم لمكانك منهم، أخوتنا من بني المطلب أعطيتهم وتركتنا ، وإنما نحن وهم منك بمنزلة واحد. فقال : إنهم لم يفارقوني في جاهليّة ولا إسلام، وإنما بنو هاشم وبنو المطلب شيء واحد، ثمّ شبك بين أصابعه» (١) ، ومثله أخرجه البخاري، وأحمد، وإسحاق بن راهويه ، والبزار ، وأبو يعلي، والطحاوي، وابن ماجة، وغيرهم. [٤]قوله : خيبر ؛ على وزن جعفر موضع بقرب المدينة، وكان فتحها سنة سبع من الهجرة. [٠] قوله : من أولاد عبد شمس ؛ فإنّه أمير المؤمنين، وصهر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عثمان بن عفان بن أبي العاص بن أمية بن عبد شمس. [٦] قوله : من أولادِ نوفل ؛ فإنّه جبير مصغراً ابن مطعم، على وزن منعم، ابن عدي بن نوفل بن عبد مناف. (١) في سنن النسائي الكبرى (۳): (٤٨) ، و(السنن الصغير (۳ : ۲۰۸)، وغيرها.
[3] His statement: 'When he divided... etc.' Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i narrated from Jubayr ibn Mut'im who said: 'When the Messenger of Allah divided the share of Dhawi al-Qurba from Khaybar between Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, I and Uthman came and said: O Messenger of Allah, these are Banu Hashim, we do not deny their virtue due to your place among them, but our brothers from Banu al-Muttalib – you gave them and left us, while we and they are in one position from you. He said: They did not abandon me in Jahiliyyah or Islam; Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib are one thing, and he interlaced his fingers.' Similar was narrated by al-Bukhari, Ahmad, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, al-Bazzar, Abu Ya'la, al-Tahawi, Ibn Majah, and others. [4] His statement: 'Khaybar' – on the weight of Ja'far, a place near Madinah; its conquest was in the year seven of the Hijrah. [0] His statement: 'From the children of Abd Shams' – he is the Commander of the Faithful, the son-in-law of the Prophet, Uthman ibn Affan ibn Abi al-As ibn Umayyah ibn Abd Shams. [6] His statement: 'From the children of Nawfal' – he is Jubayr (diminutive) ibn Mut'im, on the weight of Mun'im, ibn Adi ibn Nawfal ibn Abd Manaf. (1) In Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra (3:48) and al-Sunan al-Saghir (3:208) and others.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage provides a hadith as proof (dalil) for the ruling that the share of Dhawi al-Qurba (close relatives) from the khums is to be given exclusively to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, excluding other branches of Quraysh like Banu Umayyah and Banu Nawfal. The hadith from Jubayr ibn Mut'im reports that when the Prophet distributed the khums of Khaybar, he gave it to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib. Jubayr and Uthman (who were from Banu Abd Shams and Banu Nawfal respectively) objected, noting that they were also relatives. The Prophet responded that Banu al-Muttalib had supported him in both Jahiliyyah and Islam, unlike the others, and declared that Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib are considered as one entity. This establishes the Hanafi position that the 'Dhawi al-Qurba' mentioned in the Quran (Al-Anfal: 41) refers specifically to these two clans. The commentary also clarifies the identity of Khaybar and provides notes on the lineage of Uthman and Jubayr. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah as transmitted in the major hadith collections.

Key Terms
خمس ذوي القربى (khums dhawi al-qurba): One-fifth of the spoils (ghanimah) allocated to the close relatives of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as per Quran 8:41.
بنو هاشم (Banu Hashim): The clan of the Prophet, descendants of Hashim ibn Abd Manaf.
بنو المطلب (Banu al-Muttalib): The clan of al-Muttalib ibn Abd Manaf, the Prophet's uncle's clan; they supported the Prophet and are included with Banu Hashim in this share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith, why did the Prophet exclude Banu Abd Shams and Banu Nawfal from the share of Dhawi al-Qurba?
A) Because they were not from Quraysh
B) Because they left him in both Jahiliyyah and Islam
C) Because they did not ask for it
D) Because they were not present at the time of distribution

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school confines the khums share for Dhawi al-Qurba to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib based on the explicit practice of the Prophet.

The Khums Share for the Prophet's Relatives: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute over khums share for Prophet's relatives after his death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the khums share for dhawi al-qurba after the Prophet's death and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the dalil from the hadith.

Core Idea
The Prophet gave khums to his supporters, not merely his kin; Hanafis say the right ends with him.

This passage presents a key inter-madhhab dispute over the distribution of the khums (one-fifth of war booty) allocated to the Prophet's near relatives. The Shafi'i school holds that the share for relatives continues after the Prophet's death, while the Hanafi school argues that it was specific to his lifetime based on the reason of support. Understanding this dispute requires careful analysis of the hadith and the principle of 'illa (effective cause).

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
What exactly is the dispute? Determine whether the khums share for dhawi al-qurba (near relatives of the Prophet) should be allocated after his death or not.
The Shafi'i position: allocate it as the Prophet did. The Hanafi position: the relatives are no longer entitled unless poor.
2
Examine the Hanafi dalil
Read the Prophet's statement 'they did not abandon me' and identify the 'illa (effective cause) that Hanafis derive: the support and companionship of Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.
This 'illa is specific to the Prophet's lifetime.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-dalil
The Shafi'i school argues that the Prophet's division sets a permanent precedent; the category of relatives is established in the Quran and Sunnah as a perpetual right.
They point to the general command in the Quran 8:41.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
How do Hanafis rebut the Shafi'i position? They cite the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar, who did not allocate a separate share to relatives after the Prophet's death.
The Hanafi view is that the Prophet's action was based on ijtihad and a specific reason, not a general rule.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Hanafis distribute the khums among orphans, poor, and travelers only; Shafi'is give one-fifth of khums to the Prophet's relatives.
This affects the distribution of booty in Islamic finance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان لعبد مناف أربعة بنين هاشم والمطلب، وعبد شمس، ونوفل، [٤] ولما قسم رسول الله غنائم خيبر ، قسم خمس ذوي القربى بين بني هاشم، وبني المطلب، وكان عثمان له من أولاد عبد شمس، وجبير بن مطعم من [٦] أولادِ نوفل وعليهما الصلاة والسلام، وقد بسط الكلام فيه أصحاب السير، وقد لخصت ما ذكرت من «المواهب اللدنية» وشروحه. [1] قوله : وعبد شمس ؛ هو والد أمية مصغراً وعبد العزى وغيرهما، وبنو أمية من أولاده، ومنهم معاوية وغيره من خلفاء بني أمية. [٤]قوله : خيبر ؛ على وزن جعفر موضع بقرب المدينة، وكان فتحها سنة سبع من الهجرة. [0] قوله : من أولاد عبد شمس ؛ فإنّه أمير المؤمنين، وصهر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عثمان بن عفان بن أبي العاص بن أمية بن عبد شمس. ثم قال: فكلما رسول الله الله فقالا : لا ننكر فضل بني هاشم لمكانك الذي وضعك الله فيهم، ولكن نحن وأصحابنا من بني المطلب إليك في النسب سواء فما بالك ) أعطيتهم وحرمتنا، فقال : «إِنَّهُم لَمْ يُفَارِقُونَي فِي الجَاهِلِيَّةِ وَلَا فِي الإِسلام، وشبك بين نَ أَصَابِعِه» (١) ، فالشَّافِعِي (۲) له يقسمه كما قسم النبي . (۱) ونحن نقول له [١] قوله : سواء ؛ فإنّ المطّلب وعبد شمس ونوفل كلّهم أبناء عبد مناف، فأولاد هؤلاء إليك في القرابة والنسبة متساوية. [٢]قوله : فما بالك ؛ أي حالك وخيالك ، يعني لم أعطيت الخمس لبني المطلب، وجعلتنا محرومين مع المساواة بيننا وبينهم بالنسبة إليك. [٣] قوله : لم يفارقوني ؛ يعني لم يزالوا موافقين معي وناصرين لي في الإسلام وما قبله، وأما أولاد عبد شمس ونوفل، فكانت بينهم وبين بني هاشم مخاصمات ومنازعات، وهذا يدل على أن تقسيم خمس ذوي القربي كان مفوضاً إلى رأي النبي ، وكانت له الخيرة في إعطاء قوم ومنع قوم من ذوي قرباه بحسب ما يراه من [٤] قوله : وشبك ؛ ماض من التشبيك ، يعني شبك النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أصابع يديه ؛ أي المصلحة. أدخل أصابع إحدى اليدين في الأخرى إشارة إلى كمال الاختلاط والتواصل. [0] قوله : كما قسم النبي ؛ وهو أن يجعل الخمس على خمسة سهام، سهم لليتامى، وسهم للمساكين، وسهم لأبناء السبيل، وسهم للخليفة، وسهم لذوي قربى النبي ؛ أي بني هاشم وبني المطلب. [٦] قوله : ونحن نقول ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين نازعه عثمان وجبير علل إعطاؤه لبني هاشم وبني المطّلب بالصحبة والتناصر ، وهو مقتصر على حياته ، فبعد وفاته لم يبق لهم استحقاق إلا بالفقر، وبه عمل أبو بكر وعمر وغيرهما كما مرّ.
Abd Manaf had four sons: Hashim, al-Muttalib, Abd Shams, and Nawfal. [4] When the Messenger of Allah divided the spoils of Khaybar, he divided the khums of the near relatives among Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib. Uthman was from the children of Abd Shams, and Jubayr ibn Mut'im from the children of Nawfal - peace be upon them both. The writers of biographies have elaborated on this, and I have summarized from 'al-Mawahib al-Ladunniyya' and its commentaries. [1] His saying: 'and Abd Shams': he is the father of Umayya (diminutive) and Abd al-Uzza and others; Banu Umayya are from his children, among them Mu'awiya and other Umayyad caliphs. [2] His saying: 'and Nawfal': with fatha on nun, sukun on waw, and fatha on fa; from his children are Adi, Amir, and others. [3] His saying: 'When he divided...': Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i narrated from Jubayr ibn Mut'im that he said: 'When the Messenger of Allah divided the share of the near relatives from Khaybar among Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, I and Uthman came and said: O Messenger of Allah! These are Banu Hashim - we do not deny their virtue due to your position among them. But our brothers from Banu al-Muttalib: you gave them and left us, while we and they are of the same status in relation to you. He said: They did not abandon me in Jahiliyya or Islam. Indeed, Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib are one thing. Then he intertwined his fingers.' (1) Similar is narrated by al-Bukhari, Ahmad, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, al-Bazzar, Abu Ya'la, al-Tahawi, Ibn Majah, and others. [4] His saying: 'Khaybar': on the pattern of Ja'far, a place near Medina; its conquest was in the seventh year of Hijra. [0] His saying: 'from the children of Abd Shams': he is the Commander of the Faithful and son-in-law of the Prophet, Uthman ibn Affan ibn Abi al-As ibn Umayya ibn Abd Shams. [6] His saying: 'from the children of Nawfal': he is Jubayr (diminutive) ibn Mut'im (on the pattern of mun'im), ibn Adi ibn Nawfal ibn Abd Manaf.

Then he said: so both spoke to the Messenger of Allah: 'We do not deny the virtue of Banu Hashim because of the position Allah has placed you among them, but we and our companions from Banu al-Muttalib are equal to you in lineage. So what is the matter that you gave them and deprived us?' He said: 'They did not abandon me in Jahiliyya or Islam,' and he intertwined his fingers. (1) So al-Shafi'i (2) is of the opinion that one should divide it as the Prophet divided it. (1) And we say to him: [1] His saying: 'equal': indeed al-Muttalib, Abd Shams, and Nawfal are all sons of Abd Manaf, so the children of these are equal to you in kinship and relation. [2] His saying: 'what is the matter': meaning your situation, i.e., why did you give the khums to Banu al-Muttalib and made us deprived despite equality in relation to you? [3] His saying: 'they did not abandon me': meaning they have always been in agreement with me and supporting me in Islam and before it. As for the children of Abd Shams and Nawfal, there were conflicts and disputes between them and Banu Hashim. This indicates that the division of the khums of the near relatives was delegated to the opinion of the Prophet, and he had the choice to give or withhold from his relatives according to what he saw of [4] His saying: 'intertwined': past tense of tashbik, meaning the Prophet intertwined the fingers of his hands, i.e., the benefit. He inserted the fingers of one hand into the other indicating complete intermingling and connection. [0] His saying: 'as the Prophet divided': that he made the khums into five shares: a share for orphans, a share for the poor, a share for travelers, a share for the caliph, and a share for the near relatives of the Prophet - i.e., Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib. [6] His saying: 'and we say...': the summary is that when Uthman and Jubayr disputed with him, the Prophet justified his giving to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib by companionship and mutual support, and this is limited to his lifetime. After his death, they have no entitlement except due to poverty. Abu Bakr, Umar, and others acted upon this as has passed.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the distribution of the khums share allocated to the Prophet's near relatives (dhawi al-qurba). The author explains the background: the Prophet divided the khums from Khaybar among Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, to the exclusion of other relatives like Uthman (from Banu Umayya, a sub-clan of Abd Shams) and Jubayr (from Banu Nawfal). When Uthman and Jubayr objected, the Prophet justified his decision by stating that Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib had stood by him throughout, while the others had not. The Shafi'i school holds that this allocation sets a precedent that should be followed after the Prophet's death as well, continuing to give a share to the relatives. The Hanafi school, however, argues that the Prophet's decision was based on his own ijtihad, and the reason ('illa) was their support and companionship, which was specific to his lifetime. Therefore, after his death, the relatives are not entitled to a separate share unless they are poor; instead, the khums is distributed among the general categories of orphans, the poor, and travelers. This was the practice of Abu Bakr, Umar, and later caliphs, which the Hanafis follow. The commentary highlights that the Prophet's statement 'they did not abandon me' indicates the cause was their alliance, not mere kinship.

Key Terms
خمس (khums): One-fifth of war booty allocated to specific categories: Allah, the Prophet, relatives, orphans, poor, travelers.
ذوي القربى (dhawi al-qurba): The near relatives of the Prophet, specifically Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib according to Hanafis.
بني هاشم (Banu Hashim): The clan of the Prophet, descendants of Hashim ibn Abd Manaf.
بني المطلب (Banu al-Muttalib): The clan of al-Muttalib ibn Abd Manaf, closely allied with Banu Hashim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Prophet allocate the khums share to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib instead of other relatives?
A) Because they were the closest in kinship
B) Because they supported him and did not abandon him
C) Because they were poor
D) Because they asked for it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the khums share for the Prophet's relatives was specific to his lifetime due to the 'illa of support; after his death, the relatives are not entitled unless poor.

The Effect of Prohibition on Legal Validity: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Seizure by Infidels
Hanafi response to Shafi'i on prohibition of seizure by infidels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between inherent and external ugliness in prohibitions and evaluate how this distinction affects the legal validity of acts, using the case of seizure by infidels as an example.

Core Idea
A prohibition due to an external factor does not invalidate the act; the act remains valid but disliked.

The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ on whether a prohibited act can still produce its legal effects. This dispute turns on whether the prohibition is inherent to the act or arises from an external circumstance. In the case of property seized by infidels, the Hanafi school argues that the prohibition is due to the inviolability of property, which is contingent on being under Muslim protection. When that protection is lost, the property reverts to its original permissibility, and the seizure becomes valid in worldly terms, albeit disliked. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school distinguishes between inherent ugliness (qubh li-ʿaynihi) and external ugliness (qubh li-ghayrihi). In the case of seizure by infidels, the prohibition is external, so the act is valid in worldly terms but disliked.
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a prohibition on a legal act is presumed inherent, thus the seizure is invalid and does not produce ownership.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response relies on the Quranic verse (He created for you all that is in the earth) indicating that property is inherently permissible. Inviolability is contingent on the ability to benefit, which is lost when property is under infidel control.
Also distinguishes between sensory and legal acts.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis rule that the infidel owns the property (valid but disliked in the Hereafter), while Shafi'is rule no ownership transfers.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, supported by the majority of Hanafi scholars and evidence from the Quran and rational analysis.

And the inherently ugly (qubh li-'aynihi) does not produce a legal ruling; that is, ownership. We said: They only own [the property] due to their seizure of property that is not legally inviolable, for the Law rules that the offer and acceptance that exist physically are linked in a legal connection. Thus a legal meaning occurs that has an effect on the buyer's ownership; that meaning is sale, such that if offer and acceptance occur in an inappropriate context, the Law does not consider it a sale, and if it occurs with an option, the Law rules that the sale exists without ownership attaching to it, so the legal existence is established. End. And in al-Talwih: 'The conclusion: The prohibition of a sensory act is, when unqualified, interpreted as ugly in itself (qubh li-'aynihi), i.e., for its essence or part thereof; by virtue of a contextual indication it is interpreted as ugly for another reason (qubh li-ghayrihi). That other, if it is a quality inherent in the prohibited act, it is like the inherently ugly; if it is a separate external factor, then not. And the prohibition of a legal act is, when unqualified, interpreted as ugly for another reason, and by virtue of a contextual indication as inherently ugly. Al-Shafi'i held the opposite. The consequence is whether the rulings attach to it or not.' [1] His statement: 'The inherently ugly' – whether a sensory act or a legal act, evidence indicates that the ugliness is inherent. [2] His statement: 'does not produce a legal ruling' – this is agreed upon between us and al-Shafi'i. This differs from the ugly for another reason, for it produces ownership, and its effects attach, albeit with dislike – like a sale during the Friday adhan. [3] His statement: 'We said...' etc.; this is a response to al-Shafi'i's argument, which has been detailed previously. Its summary: The prohibition of seizure is not inherent but for another reason. It cannot be said: This contradicts the consensus-based rule that prohibition of sensory acts necessitates inherent ugliness; for we say: That rule is qualified by the absence of evidence to the contrary, and here evidence exists indicating that the ugliness of seizure is for another reason, even though it is a sensory act, so its effects attach. (1) from al-Tawdih (1:414). (2) End from al-Talwih (1:415). Book of Jihad / Chapter on Seizure by Infidels and Chapter on Musta'min. [1] (3) 425 'in their estimation, and we have no authority to impose [inviolability], so the prohibition falls away in worldly matters, since inviolability was only established as long as it is in our domain due to certainty of the ability to benefit; when the protection is removed, inviolability falls.' [1] His statement: 'in their estimation' – objection: The claim is that their seizure is a cause of ownership in reality, but the evidence only indicates that it is a cause in their estimation. If this were correct, it would necessitate their ownership of our free persons and the permissibility of our blood to them, because they also believe that to be permissible. The answer: The basis of the issue is not their belief in permissibility, but rather inviolability. Inviolability in persons is reinforced by freedom reinforced by Islam, so it cannot be nullified; property cannot be analogized to persons, for property is inherently permissible, unlike a human being who is created an owner of things, honorable and protected in himself. [2] His statement: 'and we have no authority' – i.e., the people of Islam have no authority to compel them to consider the property inviolable. [3] His statement: 'so the prohibition falls' – i.e., the prohibition on seizure falls for them regarding worldly rulings due to the absence of commitment and compulsion, although it remains in the Hereafter. [4] His statement: 'since inviolability' – thus in some copies with 'idh' as a causative, and in some with 'wa al-'ismah' with the initial waw, and in some 'aw'; the latter is correct, agreeing with the wording of 'Tanqih' by the commentator and the presentation of the author of al-Talwih as mentioned. [0] His statement: 'was only established...' etc.; its clarification according to al-Hidayah and its commentaries: The verse (He created for you all that is in the earth) and similar indicate that property is not inviolable for any person originally; rather, everything is such that every person can benefit from it. Inviolability in property is established only for whoever it is established – Muslim or non-Muslim – contrary to the necessity of the owner's ability to benefit and fulfill their need. For if it were not so, everyone would have a way to attack any property, and the owner would not attain benefit. When the ability to benefit is removed by the seizure of the infidels, it becomes permissible as it was originally.
(1) al-Hidayah and al-'Inayah (6:5-6). (2) Surah al-Baqarah, part of verse 29. With the presence of evidence.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the effect of a prohibition (nahy) on the legal validity of an act, specifically the seizure of property by non-Muslims in the context of jihad. The Hanafi position is that a prohibition can be either inherent (qubh li-'aynihi) or due to an external factor (qubh li-ghayrihi). If a prohibition is due to an external factor, the act remains valid but is disliked (makruh), and its legal effects (such as ownership) still attach. The Shafi'i position, according to the text, is the opposite: a prohibition on a legal act is presumed to be inherent unless evidence indicates otherwise. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the forbidden act produces its legal effects. The Hanafi school argues that the prohibition on seizure (istila') by infidels is not inherently evil but is due to the inviolability (ismah) of the property. However, when property is in the possession of infidels and no longer under Muslim protection, the inviolability is lost, and the property becomes permissible for them to own. The text addresses a potential objection that this reasoning would also allow their ownership of free Muslims, but it distinguishes between property and persons: property is inherently permissible (mubah fi al-asl), while free Muslims are inherently protected and inviolable. Therefore, the rule for property does not apply to persons. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the prohibition of a sensory act is presumed to be inherent only in the absence of evidence to the contrary; here, evidence (the verse and the nature of property) indicates that the ugliness is for another reason. Thus, the seizure produces legal ownership for the infidels in worldly matters, even though it remains sinful in the Hereafter.

Key Terms
القبيح لعينه (al-qabīḥ li-ʿaynihi): An act that is inherently ugly or prohibited due to its essence or a part of it.
القبيح لغيره (al-qabīḥ li-ghayrihi): An act that is ugly not in itself but due to an external factor or circumstance.
العصمة (al-ʿiṣma): Legal inviolability or protection that prevents others from taking property or harming a person without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a prohibition that is due to an external factor (qubh li-ghayrihi) on a legal act?
A) The act is invalid and no legal effects arise.
B) The act is valid but disliked, and legal effects arise.
C) The act is valid and recommended.
D) The act is voidable at the discretion of the parties.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a prohibition due to an external factor does not nullify the act; the seizure by infidels is valid in this world but disliked.

Tax Rates on Irrigated Land, Vineyards, and Gardens; The Cubit Measurement Dispute
Defines tax rates, jarib area, and cubit measurement disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi tax rates for different land types (irrigated, vineyard, palm grove, garden), explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on garden tax assessment, and identify the two cubit definitions (cloth cubit vs surveying cubit) with their respective measurements.

Core Idea
Land tax varies by crop and tree density; the cubit used for measurement differs between cloth and surveying.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines the tax rates for different types of agricultural land and gardens. It also delves into the precise definition of the jarib (area unit) and the cubit, revealing a rich intra-Hanafi dispute and differences among mathematicians. Understanding these definitions is essential for correctly applying tax obligations (kharaj/'ushr) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the land types and their base tax rates
From the matn, extract the tax per jarib for irrigated land (5 dirhams) and for contiguous vineyards/palm groves (10 dirhams). Note that 'other' lands like saffron and gardens are assessed 'according to capacity'.
The rates are fixed for the first two categories; the third is flexible.
2
Understand the definition of a jarib
A jarib is defined as 60 cubits by 60 cubits. But the cubit itself is not uniform – distinguish between the cloth cubit (used for textiles) and the surveying cubit (used for land).
The area is 3600 square cubits, but the absolute size depends on which cubit is used.
3
Analyze the cubit measurement disputes
For the cloth cubit: some say 6 fists, others 7. For the surveying cubit: majority say 7 fists plus a raised thumb on each fist, but there are variant opinions. Also, ancients said 32 fingers per cubit, later scholars said 24.
The finger itself is defined as 6 barleycorns placed concave to concave to avoid gaps.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on garden tax
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that a garden (bustan) with scattered trees is taxed according to its capacity (ma yutiq), while Abu Yusuf says it cannot exceed the vine tax (10 dirhams per jarib). No explicit proof is given; the matter is reported as an ikhtilaf.
Practical consequence: under Muhammad's view, a high-yield garden could be taxed more than 10 dirhams.
5
Note the mathematical precision in defining the finger
The finger is 6 barleycorns placed with their concave sides together. The commentator explains that this placement prevents gaps that would reduce the actual length, ensuring accurate measurement.
This level of detail shows care in establishing standard units for legal obligations.

Source Text

And the irrigated jarib is five dirhams, and the jarib of vine or date palm that is contiguous is double that, and for other than that like saffron and a garden, what it can bear. The jarib is sixty cubits by sixty cubits. In the books of fiqh, the cloth cubit is seven fists, and the surveying cubit is seven fists and a raised thumb. According to the calculators, the cubit is twenty-four fingers. [Footnote 1] His saying: 'the irrigated jarib' – it (with fatha on ra and ta) is cucumber, melon, watermelon, eggplant, and what runs in their course, and greens other than moist vegetables, as in 'al-Mughrib' and others. [Footnote 2] His saying: 'contiguous' – it is a state of the vine and date palm, so if the grape and date trees are scattered such that their middle is cultivated, then nothing is on them, as in 'al-Bahr'. [Footnote 3] His saying: 'and for other than that' – meaning for other than what we mentioned, no assessment from the Companions is established. [Footnote 4] His saying: 'and a garden' – it (with damm) is any land surrounded by a wall containing scattered trees under which cultivation is possible. If the trees are dense such that cultivation is impossible, then according to Muhammad an assessment is placed on it according to what it can bear, and Abu Yusuf said: It is not increased beyond the vine. As in 'al-Ikhtiyar' and 'al-Bada'i'. [0] His saying: 'and in the books of fiqh...' – know that the cubit is sometimes used and intended to be the cloth cubit, i.e. the one with which cloth is measured. They differed on its amount: what is mentioned in 'Fath al-Qadir' and many books is that it is six fists without raising a thumb. What is mentioned in 'al-Fatawa al-Walwaljiyya', 'al-Inaya', 'al-Zahiriyya', and others is that it is seven fists, and what is meant by a fist is four fingers joined together. The finger is six barleycorns placed with their bellies to each other. And it is sometimes used and intended to be the surveying cubit, which is used to measure land. Its amount according to what is in 'Fath al-Qadir' and 'al-Tatarkhaniyya' quoting from 'al-Ghiyathiyya' and 'al-Sayrafiyya' is seven fists with a raised thumb above each fist, and similar is in many books, and what is meant by a raised thumb is the thumb. And it is said: it is seven fists with the raised thumb only in the seventh time, and it is said: seven fists with a laid thumb at each fist. Hence the jurists differed on what is intended by the cubit in determining the tenth on the tenth which is for flowing water. It is said the first is considered, and it is said the second. Let its detail be sought from 'The Investigation of the Tenth on the Tenth' from 'al-Si'aya'. And the calculators differed on the estimation of the cubit: the ancients said it is thirty-two fingers, and the later ones said it is twenty-four fingers. The finger according to all is six barleycorns placed with their bellies to their backs, and each barleycorn is the size of six horsehairs from the tail of a Turkish horse. Let the detail of this investigation be sought from 'The Investigation of Tayammum' from 'al-Si'aya', and from my treatise related to the investigation of seven barleycorn widths, occurring in the commentary on 'Talkhis al-Jaghmim' in astronomy, called 'al-Ifada al-Khatira'. [1] His saying: 'placed' – Imam al-Din ibn Lutf Allah, the geometer from Lahore and Delhi, said: It is restricted thus because if they were placed lengthwise contiguous in a complete extension or widthwise with their backs against backs, the number of the six barleycorns of the finger's width would decrease and the calculation would be corrupted. That is because the barleycorn is curved in shape. If the convex of one is placed against the convex of another, they only touch near a point. Similarly, if the concave is placed against the concave, only the two ends meet, leaving a gap between them, thus decreasing the number of the estimated finger's barleycorns. End quote.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Wiqaya regarding land taxes (kharaj or 'ushr). The matn gives specific tax rates per jarib (a unit of area, defined as 60x60 cubits). For irrigated land (al-jarib al-ratiba) growing crops like cucumbers, melons, eggplant, etc., the tax is 5 dirhams per jarib. For vineyards or date palms that are contiguous (trees covering the land so that nothing else can be grown), the tax is double (10 dirhams). For anything else, such as saffron or a garden (bustan) with scattered trees under which cultivation is possible, the tax is assessed according to its capacity (ma yutiq). The commentator clarifies the definitions: the jarib is 60x60 cubits, but the exact length of a cubit (dhira') is disputed. He presents two main cubits: the cloth cubit (dhira' al-kirbas) used for measuring cloth, and the surveying cubit (dhira' al-misaha) used for land. The cloth cubit is said by some to be 6 fists (qabdah) and by others 7 fists; a fist is 4 fingers. The surveying cubit is often defined as 7 fists plus a raised thumb (insba' qa'im) on each fist or only on the seventh. There is also a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the tax on a garden: Muhammad says to assess it according to capacity, while Abu Yusuf says it should not exceed the tax of a vineyard (i.e., 10 dirhams). The passage also records a dispute among mathematicians about the number of fingers in a cubit: ancients said 32, later ones said 24. A finger is defined as 6 barleycorns placed side by side, but the commentator emphasizes that the barleycorns must be placed with their concave sides together to avoid gaps. This level of detail is typical of later Hanafi scholarship (mutakhkhirin) and shows the careful attention to measurement standards that affect land tax calculations.

Key Terms
الجريب (al-jarīb): A unit of land area equal to 60 cubits by 60 cubits (3600 square cubits). Used for tax assessment.
الذراع (al-dhirāʿ): A unit of length; here distinguished between cloth cubit (ذراع الكرباس) and surveying cubit (ذراع المساحة). The cloth cubit is 6 or 7 fists; the surveying cubit is 7 fists plus a thumb.
القبضة (al-qabḍa): A fist; a unit of length equal to the width of four fingers joined together.
الأصبع (al-iṣbaʿ): A finger; a unit of length equal to six barleycorns placed side by side with their concave sides together.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, what is the tax per jarib for contiguous vines or date palms?
A) 5 dirhams
B) 10 dirhams
C) According to capacity
D) 20 dirhams

Key Takeaway
Land tax rates in Hanafi fiqh are defined per jarib (area unit) and vary by crop type and tree density; precise measurement standards (cubit definitions) are essential for accurate assessment.

Maximum and Variation of Kharaj: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Increasing Land Tax
Intra-Hanafi dispute on increasing kharaj when land capacity improves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum kharaj as half the produce, explain the mandatory reduction when land cannot bear it, and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf (no increase) and Muhammad (increase allowed) with their respective proofs.

Core Idea
Maximum kharaj is half; reduction is mandatory; increase is disputed—Abu Yusuf forbids it, Muhammad permits it.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with the maximum amount of land tax (kharaj) and whether it can change over time. Caliph 'Umar set half the produce as the utmost limit, and reduction is required if the land cannot bear the assessment. However, the school splits on whether the tax can be increased when the land's productivity rises. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists balance textual precedent (practice of 'Umar) with analogical reasoning (qiyas).

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational ruling
Find the matn statement that establishes the maximum kharaj as half the produce. The commentator explains this is the utmost capacity of land.
The matn says: 'Wa nisf al-kharij ghayat al-taqah'.
2
Understand the reduction rule
Note that if in any year the land cannot yield enough to pay the assessed tax, the assessment is reduced. This is supported by 'Umar's instruction to his officials.
Reduction is mandatory, not optional.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The third part addresses increase when land capacity improves. Identify the two positions: Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa) say no increase; Muhammad says increase is permitted.
The text explicitly states both views.
4
Analyze each position's proof
Abu Yusuf relies on the established practice of 'Umar who never increased the tax despite knowing of increased capacity. Muhammad relies on the 'illa (effective cause) of capacity: just as reduction follows decreased capacity, increase should follow increased capacity.
The proofs are dalil naqli (Umar's action) vs qiyas.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The commentary mentions that al-Kafi states the correct view is that of Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa). This becomes the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Tarjih authority: al-Kafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصف الخارج غاية الطاقة، ونقص إن لم تطق وظيفتها، ولا يزاد إن أطاقت عند أبي يوسف، وجاز عند محمد له. [1] قوله: ونصف؛ مبتدأ خبر قوله: «غاية الطاقة»؛ وحاصله إن نهاية طاقة الأرض أن يكون الواجب نصف ما تخرجه الأرض، فيجوز توظيف ما هو أقل منه كالربع والخمس ونحو ذلك، ولا تجوز الزيادة على النّصف لا في خراج المقاسمة، ولا في الخراج الموظف، كما لا تجوز الزيادة على مقدار ما ثبت عن عمر وأصحابه في جريب الكرم، وغيره. كذا في «البحر». [2] قوله: ونقص؛ يعني: إذا وظفت على الأرض وظيفة فلم تطق في سنة من السنين بقلة نمائها، وما يخرج منها نقصت الوظيفة. يدل عليه قول عمر لحذيفة وعثمان بن حنيف، وقد كان بعثهما لمساحة أراضي العراق ووضع الخراج عليها: «كيف فعلتما، أتخافان أن تكونا حملتما الأرض ما لا تطيق، قالا: حملناها أمراً هي له مطيقة ما فيها كبير فضل، قال: انظرا أن تكونا حملتماها ما لا تطيق، قالا: لا)، أخرجه البخاري وغيره. [3] قوله: ولا يزاد؛ يعني إن وظفت على الأرض وظيفة حسب طاقتها فزادت طاقتها بزيادة ما خرج منها، فعند محمد تجوز الزيادة على الوظيفة كما يجوز النقصان؛ لأنّ الاعتبار للطاقة، فبزيادتها يزاد في الوظيفة وبنقصانها ينقص. وعند أبي يوسف: لا يزاد لما ثبت عن عمر أنه لما أخبر بزيادة الطاقة لم يزد. كما أخرجه عبد الرزاق وغيره.
And half of the produce is the utmost capacity; and it is reduced if [the land] does not bear its assessment, and it is not increased if it bears [more] according to Abu Yusuf, and it is permitted according to Muhammad. [1] His statement: 'And half': a mubtada, its khabar is 'the utmost capacity'; its conclusion: the limit of the land's ability is that the obligation be half of what the land produces, so it is permissible to assess what is less than that like a quarter or a fifth and so on, and increase beyond half is not permissible, neither in kharaj muqasamah nor in fixed kharaj, just as increase beyond the amount established from 'Umar and his Companions per jarib of vineyard and others is not permissible. This is as in 'al-Bahr'. [2] His statement: 'And reduced': meaning if a tax is assessed on land and in a certain year it cannot bear [it] due to scarcity of its growth and what comes out of it, the assessment is reduced. This is supported by the statement of 'Umar to Hudhayfah and 'Uthman ibn Hunayf, when he had sent them to survey the lands of Iraq and set the kharaj on them: 'What did you do? Do you fear you have burdened the land with what it cannot bear?' They said, 'We burdened it with something it can bear, with no great surplus.' He said, 'Look lest you have burdened it with what it cannot bear.' They said, 'No.' Recorded by al-Bukhari and others. [3] His statement: 'And not increased': meaning if a tax is assessed on land according to its capacity, then its capacity increases due to increase of its produce, then according to Muhammad increase in the assessment is permissible just as reduction is permissible, because consideration is given to capacity; when it increases, the assessment is increased, and when it decreases, it is decreased. According to Abu Yusuf: it is not increased, because it is established from 'Umar that when he was informed of an increase in capacity, he did not increase. As recorded by 'Abd al-Razzaq and others.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding land tax (kharaj). It is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn first states that the maximum kharaj is half of the produce — this is the utmost capacity of land. The commentator explains that the obligation should not exceed half, whether the tax is proportional (kharaj muqasamah) or fixed. It is permissible to assess less, like a quarter or fifth. No increase is allowed beyond the rate established by Caliph 'Umar and his companions. The second part of the matn says the tax is reduced if in a given year the land cannot bear it due to poor yield. The commentary supports this with the hadith of 'Umar who instructed his officials not to overburden the land. The third part addresses when the land's capacity increases. Here there is a dispute: Imam Muhammad allows increasing the tax because the assessment is based on actual capacity — if capacity increases, the tax may increase, just as it is reduced when capacity decreases. Abu Yusuf forbids any increase, citing the consistent practice of 'Umar who never increased the kharaj even when capacity improved. This difference stems from whether the fixed rate is permanently tied to the original assessment or flexible with changing conditions.

Key Terms
خراج (kharāj): Land tax imposed on conquered lands that remain in the hands of non-Muslims or are held by Muslims with kharaj status.
طاقة (tāqah): The capacity of land to produce and bear tax; the maximum set by Caliph 'Umar is half the produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the maximum amount of kharaj that can be assessed on land?
A) The entire produce
B) Half of the produce
C) One-quarter of the produce
D) It depends on the ruler's discretion

Key Takeaway
The maximum kharaj is half the produce; reduction is mandatory when land cannot bear it, but increase is prohibited according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Kharaj Exemption for Calamities and Obligation Despite Neglect
Ruling on exemption from kharaj when land is affected by natural calamities and obligation if owner neglects cultivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between calamities that excuse kharaj and owner negligence that does not, and explain the underlying principle of potential growth.

Core Idea
Kharaj is due on land's potential, not actual crop; thus natural disasters excuse, but laziness does not.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the commentator expands on a ruling about kharaj exemptions. The matn states two situations: calamities that exempt and owner neglect that does not. The commentary clarifies the types of calamities and the key principle that kharaj is based on potential growth, unlike 'ushr. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the tax rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The matn states: (a) no kharaj if water cut off or pestilence afflicts, (b) kharaj is obligatory if owner neglects. Recognize these as two separate conditions with opposite consequences.
The first condition is an exemption, the second is a reaffirmation of obligation.
2
Define the calamity (āfah)
The commentary classifies calamities: celestial (fire, flood, extreme cold) and non-celestial unpreventable (like locusts). Preventable damages do not exempt.
Preventable damages include those from predators, monkeys, mice if fencing is possible.
3
Understand potential vs actual growth
The commentary explains that for 'ushr, actual growth matters; for kharaj, potential growth. This is the key principle that justifies obligation despite neglect.
Potential growth means the land's ability to produce, not actual production.
4
Apply the exception for owner's inability
If the owner is unable to cultivate (e.g., no strength or means), the imam may step in to lease or sharecrop to collect kharaj from the land's income.
This ensures the kharaj is paid without burdening the incapable owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خراج لو انقطع الماء عن أرضه، أو غلب عليها، أو أصاب الزرع آفة، ويجب إن عطلها مالكها [1] قوله: ولا خراج؛ أي لا يجب خراج لا خراج موظف ولا خراج مقاسمة، ولا العشر، وعدم العشر خراج المقاسمة ظاهر لتعلّق الواجب فيهما بعين الخارج، كذا في «الفتاوي الخيرية». [2] قوله: آفة؛ أي سماوية كحرق وغرق وشدّة برد، فإن كانت غير سماوية، فإن كانت مما لا يمكن الاحتراز عنها كأكل الجراد، فكذلك وإن كان الاحتراز عنها ممكناً كأكل سباع وقردة وفأرة ونحو ذلك لا يسقط الخراج، كما لا يسقط إذا هلك الخارج بعد الحصاد، كذا في «منح الغفار»، و«البحر». [3] قوله: ويجب...الخ؛ الأصلُ في هذا الباب أنّ المعتبر في وجوب العشر هو النماء التحقيقي، وفي الخراج النماء التقديري فإذا عدّل المالك الأرض الصالحة للزراعة ولم يزرع لا يجب العشر، ويجب الخراج؛ لوجود قدرته ووجوبه في الذمة بالتمكن من الزراعة. وفي «الخانية»: له في أرض الخراج سبخة لا تصلح للزراعة، أو لا يصلحها الماء، إن أمكنه إصلاحها ولم يصلح فعليه الخراج وإلا فلا، ولو عجز المالك عن الزراعة لعدم قوّته وأسبابه فللإمام أن يدفعها لغيره مزارعة؛ ليأخذ الخراج من نصيب المالك ويمسك الباقي للمالك، وإن شاء أجْرَها وأخذَ الخراج من الأجرة، وإن شاء
And no kharaj if water is cut off from his land, or if it is overwhelmed [by water], or if a pestilence afflicts the crop, and it is obligatory if its owner neglects it. [1] His statement: 'And no kharaj': meaning no kharaj is due, neither fixed kharaj nor proportional kharaj, nor the tithe; and the non-tithe in proportional kharaj is apparent because the obligation is attached to the actual produce, as in al-Fatawa al-Khayriyyah. [2] His statement: 'Pestilence': meaning celestial [calamity] like fire, flood, and extreme cold. If it is non-celestial, then if it is something that cannot be guarded against like locusts, it is likewise; but if guarding against it is possible like the eating of predators, monkeys, mice, and the like, the kharaj is not dropped, just as it is not dropped if the produce perishes after harvest, as in Manh al-Ghaffar and al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'And it is obligatory...': The principle in this chapter is that in the obligation of tithe, actual growth is considered, while in kharaj, potential growth is considered. So if the owner leaves land suitable for cultivation uncultivated, no tithe is due, but kharaj is due because of his ability and the obligation in his liability due to his ability to cultivate. In al-Khaniyyah: If he has in kharaj land a salt marsh unsuitable for cultivation, or water does not make it suitable, if he can reclaim it and does not, he is liable for kharaj; otherwise, not. And if the owner is unable to cultivate due to lack of strength and means, the imam may give it to another as muzara'ah to take the kharaj from the owner's share and keep the rest for the owner, or he may lease it and take the kharaj from the rent, or...
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with exemptions from kharaj due to natural calamities. The matn states that no kharaj is due if the land's water supply is cut off, or if the land is overwhelmed by water (like flooding), or if a pestilence (āfah) afflicts the crop. The commentary clarifies that the calamity must be 'celestial' (samāwiyyah) — acts of God like fire, flood, or extreme cold. If the calamity is non-celestial but unpreventable (like locust swarms), the exemption also applies. However, if the damage could have been prevented (e.g., animals eating the crop when fences could have been built), the kharaj remains due. Also, if the crop perishes after harvest, the kharaj is still due because the obligation was already attached to the produce. Then the matn says that kharaj is obligatory if the owner 'neglects' (ʻaṭṭalahā) the land — leaving it uncultivated without valid reason. The commentary explains the underlying principle: for tithe ('ushr), actual growth (al-namā' al-taḥqīqī) is considered — so if land is not cultivated, no tithe is due. For kharaj, potential growth (al-namā' al-taqdīrī) is considered — the land's ability to produce, not actual production. Therefore, if the land is suitable for farming and the owner leaves it idle, he must still pay kharaj because he could have cultivated it. Exception: if the land is inherently unsuitable (e.g., saline marsh) and cannot be reclaimed, then no kharaj. If the owner is physically or financially unable to cultivate, the imam may step in: either give the land to someone else as muzara'ah (sharecropping) and take the kharaj from the owner's share, or lease it and take kharaj from the rent.

Key Terms
آفة (āfah): A pestilence or calamity that afflicts crops, specifically celestial (like fire, flood, extreme cold) or non-celestial but unpreventable (like locusts).
تعطيل (taʻṭīl): Neglecting or leaving land uncultivated by the owner without valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a landowner leaves his fertile kharaj land uncultivated due to laziness, is he still obligated to pay kharaj?
A) No, because no crop was produced
B) Yes, because kharaj is based on potential growth
C) Yes, but only half the usual rate
D) No, because the land was not used

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, kharaj is a tax on land's potential productivity, so natural calamities beyond human control cancel it, but the owner's deliberate neglect does not.

Jizya on Non-Arab Idol Worshippers: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on jizya amounts with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi jizya rates for non-Arab Magians/idol worshippers (48/24/12 dirhams) and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof from the hadith of Mu'adh.

Core Idea
Jizya on non-Arab pagans is based on wealth; the flat one dinar from every adult is only for treaty jizya.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on jizya for non-Arab worshippers of idols and fire (Magians). Unlike the jizya on People of the Book, which is fixed by Quran and hadith, the jizya on these groups is derived from the general obligation to fight polytheists and from qiyas on the permissibility of enslaving them. The Hanafi school, following the practice of Umar, imposes a wealth-based jizya: 48 dirhams for the rich, 24 for the middle, and 12 for the working poor annually. Imam al-Shafi‘i, however, holds a flat one dinar per adult regardless of wealth, citing the hadith of Mu‘adh. The Hanafi response argues that Mu'adh's hadith applied only to jizya of peace (sulh), not to the jizya imposed by force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the Hanafi ruling
The act is the imposition of jizya on non-Arab Magians and idol worshippers. The Hanafi ruling is wajib with amounts varying by wealth: 48 dirhams annually for the wealthy, 24 for the middle, and 12 for the poor who work. These categories are defined in the commentary: the wealthy need not work, the middle have some wealth but still work, and the poor earn enough for needs but have no capital.
The ruling applies only to free adult males; women and children are not subject to jizya.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is two-fold: (1) the general Quranic command to fight polytheists (Q. 2:193) is not lifted for these groups, and (2) qiyas on the permissibility of enslaving them (by ijma). Since enslaving them is allowed, taking jizya as a lesser alternative is also allowed. The specific amounts come from the practice of Umar (athar).
The Shafi'i objection that women and children can be enslaved but not taxed is answered by noting they lack capacity to pay.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds a flat one dinar per year from every adult (male and female) regardless of wealth. His proof is the hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, where the Prophet ordered him to take from every adult a dinar or its equivalent, and other reports adding 'from every adult male and female'.
The Shafi'i view treats the hadith as general and not restricted to sulh.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the hadith is specific to jizya of peace (sulh) — the treaty made with the people of Yemen. Evidence: the hadith commands taking from women, which is not done in standard jizya. Therefore, the hadith does not apply to the regular jizya imposed by force, which follows the practice of Umar.
This response is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In Hanafi lands, jizya is assessed based on wealth; in Shafi'i areas, it is a flat per-capita rate. This difference has real economic implications for the dhimmi population.
Both positions are valid scholarly opinions; the Hanafi view is mufta bihi in the school.

وعند الشافعي على كل حالم وحالمة دينار، الفقير والغني سواء. حجته حديث معاذ بن جبل : «إنه أمره رسول الله حين بعثه على اليمن أن يأخذ من كل حالم ديناراً أو عدله معافر»، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي والنسائي وابن حبان، والحاكم، وغيرهم. وورد في مصنف عبد الرزاق، و «سنن الدارقطني»، و«مسند إسحاق بن راهويه»، وغيرها: «على كل حالم وحالمة». وأجاب عنه أصحابنا بأنه محمول على جزية الصلح ؛ ولهذا أمره بالأخذ من الحالمة وإن كانت لا تؤخذ منها الجزية. كذا في «الهداية».
And a non-Arab idol worshipper who appears wealthy: for each year forty-eight dirhams, and on the middle class half of that, and on a poor person who works one-fourth of that. [1] His statement: 'And a non-Arab idol worshipper who appears wealthy' – [there is a difference with al-Shafi'i here, for he does not impose it] – [the amount] for each year forty-eight dirhams: he takes four dirhams each month. According to him (Shafi'i): 'And on the middle class half of it, and on a poor person who works one-fourth of it.' [1] His statement: 'and a non-Arab idol worshipper' – with two fatha – an attribution to 'wathan' meaning a worshipper of an idol from other than the Arabs. As for the Arab polytheist, there is no jizya on him as will come. [2] His statement: 'who appears wealthy' – the sentence is an adjective for each of the three. He said in al-Kifayah: 'The apparently wealthy is the owner of much wealth who does not need to work. The middle class is one who has wealth but does not dispense with work. The worker is one who earns more than his need but has no wealth.' [3] His statement: 'according to him' – because fighting the disbelievers is obligatory by the text: 'And fight them until there is no fitnah and the religion is for Allah.' However, it is known that leaving it is permissible in the case of the People of the Book by the verse, and in the case of the Magians by the report, so what remains besides them remains on the original principle. And we say: Verily, idol worshippers it is permissible to enslave them by consensus, so it is permissible to take jizya from them just as it is permissible to take it from the Magians, who are fire worshippers. If you say: Women and children are permissible to enslave, yet jizya is not imposed on them. I say: In that case the locus is not susceptible. So everyone whose enslavement is permissible, jizya can be imposed on them if the locus is susceptible, as in al-‘Inayah. [4] His statement: 'and on the middle class half of it' – i.e., twenty-four dirhams. This detail is narrated from Umar, recorded by Ibn Abi Shaybah, Ibn Zanjawayh in Kitab al-Amwal, and Abu Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam in Kitab al-Amwal. And according to al-Shafi'i: from every adult male and female one dinar, the poor and the rich equal. His proof is the hadith of Mu’adh ibn Jabal: 'The Messenger of Allah ordered him when he sent him to Yemen to take from every adult a dinar or its equivalent in Ma’afir garments.' Recorded by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa’i, Ibn Hibban, al-Hakim, and others. And it is reported in the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq, Sunan al-Daraqutni, Musnad of Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and others: 'From every adult male and female.' Our masters answered that it is interpreted as jizya of peace; therefore he ordered taking from the adult female, even though jizya is not taken from her. As in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a legal exposition by the commentator on the ruling of jizya for non-Arab idol worshippers and Magians (fire worshippers). The Hanafi position, derived from the practice of Umar and analogical reasoning (qiyas), is that jizya is levied based on wealth: 48 dirhams annually for the wealthy, 24 for the middle class, and 12 for the poor who work. The commentary defines the categories: the wealthy are those with abundant wealth needing no work; the middle class have some wealth but still need to work; the worker (faqir) earns enough for his needs but owns no capital. The proof for imposing jizya on these groups is that fighting disbelievers is a general obligation (fard kifayah), but it is deferred for People of the Book (by Quran) and Magians (by hadith). Since idol worshippers are not excluded, they remain under the original obligation, and jizya is permitted as an alternative to fighting. The commentator also argues that since it is permissible to enslave these groups (by consensus), imposing jizya is a fortiori permissible. A potential objection — that women and children can be enslaved but not taxed — is answered by noting that the latter lack the capacity to pay (they are not 'adult free males'). The passage then records the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi‘i, who holds that jizya is a flat one dinar per year from every adult male and female, relying on the hadith of Mu‘adh ibn Jabal. The Hanafi response is that the hadith is specific to jizya of peace (sulh) in Yemen, not the general jizya of force; hence it is not binding for the standard jizya. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
وثني (wathani): A worshipper of an idol (wathan) from non-Arab peoples.
حالم (halim): An adult who has reached puberty (literally 'one who dreams'), i.e., a mature male or female.
جزية الصلح (jizyat al-sulh): A fixed amount agreed upon by treaty (sulh), as distinct from jizya imposed by force (jizya 'anwatan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi jizya on non-Arab Magians and idol worshippers is wealth-based (48/24/12 dirhams) and the Shafi'i flat-rate hadith is interpreted as applying only to treaty jizya.

No Jizya on Arab Idol Worshippers
Ruling that Arab idol worshippers are not subject to jizya, only Islam or death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on jizya for Arab idol worshippers and identify the dalil (proof) from the practice of the Prophet.

Core Idea
An Arab idol worshipper has no option of jizya—only Islam or the sword.

This lesson addresses a specific exception in the law of jizya: while many non-Muslims may pay jizya to remain on their religion, the Hanafi school rules that an Arab idol worshipper captured in war has no such option. The sole choice is conversion to Islam or execution. This ruling is derived from the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as narrated by al-Hasan al-Basri.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the acceptance of jizya from an Arab idol worshipper. The ruling: it is not permitted; rather, the captor must offer Islam or execution.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the report of al-Hasan al-Basri, who states that the Prophet fought the Arabs of the Arabian Peninsula for Islam and accepted nothing else.
This is a mursal hadith, accepted in Hanafi fiqh as proof for such rulings.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase is 'لم يقبل منهم غيره' (He accepted nothing else from them). This explicitly excludes jizya, indicating that only Islam was acceptable.
4
Note the scope of the ruling
This ruling applies specifically to Arab idol worshippers (wathani 'arabi) from the Arabian Peninsula. It does not apply to non-Arab idol worshippers or to People of the Book.
5
Practical consequence
In practice, if an Arab polytheist is captured, the imam must give them the choice: embrace Islam or be executed. Jizya cannot be imposed or accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲] قوله : لا على وثني عربي ؛ فقد قال الحسن البصري : «قاتل رسولُ الله أهل هذه الجزيرة من العرب إلى الإسلام لم يقبل منهم غيره، وكان أفضل الجهاد، ثم كان جهادٌ آخر في شأن أهل الكتاب ) ، أخرجه عنه أبو الشيخ، وابن أبي شيبة.
[2] His statement: 'Not on an Arab idol worshipper' – for al-Hasan al-Basri said: 'The Messenger of Allah fought the people of this peninsula from the Arabs for Islam, accepting nothing else from them, and that was the best jihad. Then there was another jihad concerning the People of the Book.' This was recorded from him by Abu al-Shaykh and Ibn Abi Shaybah.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a brief commentary on a line of the matn that states no jizya is taken from an Arab idol worshipper if captured. The commentator cites the statement of al-Hasan al-Basri, a prominent tabi‘i, who reports that the Prophet (peace be upon him) fought the Arab polytheists of the Arabian Peninsula exclusively to bring them to Islam; he did not accept jizya from them. This indicates that the choice for an Arab idol worshipper is either Islam or the sword. This ruling is based on the sunnah (practice of the Prophet). Later, after Islam was established in Arabia, jihad against the People of the Book (Jews and Christians) took a different form, where jizya became an option. The hadith of Hasan al-Basri is a mursal report, but accepted as proof in this context. The Hanafi school holds that Arab idol worshippers have no option of jizya; they must embrace Islam or face execution. This is contrasted with non-Arab idol worshippers and Magians, who may pay jizya.

Key Terms
وثني عربي (wathani 'arabi): An Arab idol worshipper, i.e., a polytheist from the Arabian Peninsula.
جزيرة العرب (jazirat al-'arab): The Arabian Peninsula, whose inhabitants were fought by the Prophet exclusively for Islam.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that Arab idol worshippers have no option of jizya; they must accept Islam or face execution, based on the Prophet's practice.
