Ruling on Stipend ('Ata) Before Possession
Ruling on ownership of stipend before receipt and upon death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a stipend is not owned before receipt and apply it to transactions, including the exception for land grants.

Core Idea
A stipend from the treasury is a gift, not owned until received, so it cannot be inherited or sold before possession.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in Islamic public finance: the stipend ('ata) granted by the state to officials such as judges, muftis, and teachers. According to the Hanafi school, such stipends are treated as gifts (sila) that do not confer ownership until physically received. This has important implications for inheritance and transactions. The passage also highlights an exception for land grants given as absolute ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal nature of a stipend
The author classifies the stipend as a 'sila' (gift). Understand that in Hanafi fiqh, a sila is a non-binding grant that does not transfer ownership until delivery.
Sila differs from hiba which requires offer and acceptance; sila is revocable before possession.
2
Determine the ruling on ownership before receipt
The text states 'fa la yumlaku qabla al-qabd' — the recipient does not own the stipend before taking physical possession. This means the right is inchoate.
Consequently, the stipend is not part of the recipient's estate upon death.
3
Apply the ruling to transactions
Since the stipend is not owned, it cannot be inherited, sold, gifted, or divided before possession. These transactions are invalid if attempted.
This applies to all monthly or yearly stipends.
4
Distinguish the exception for land grants
If the Imam gives land as absolute ownership (tamlik), then full ownership rights apply immediately. The stipend rule does not apply to such grants.
This exception is detailed in al-Qadi Muhammad's treatise on land rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه صلة ، فلا يملك قبل القبض، ويسقط بالموت ، وأهل العطاء في زماننا القاضي، والمفتي، والمدرس. والله أعلم.
قوله : فلا يملك قبل القبض ؛ فلا يجري فيه الإرث ولا يجوز بيعه وهبته وقسمته ونحو ذلك قبل القبض، وهذا في كلّ عطاء شهري أو سنوي، وأما الأراضي التي أعطاها الإمام تمليكاً، فيجري فيه جميع ما يجري في الملك على ما فصله القاضي محمد أعلى الله مؤلّف «كشاف اصطلاحات الفنون في رسالته «أحكام الأراضي».
For it is a gift, so it is not owned before receipt, and it is cancelled upon death. The recipients of stipends in our time are the judge, the mufti, and the teacher. And Allah knows best. His statement: 'So it is not owned before receipt'; so inheritance does not apply to it, nor is its sale, gift, division, and the like permissible before receipt. This applies to every monthly or yearly stipend. As for lands that the Imam gave as ownership, everything that applies to ownership applies there, as detailed by al-Qadi Muhammad—may Allah elevate him—author of 'Kashshaf Istilahat al-Funun' in his treatise 'Ahkam al-Arāḍī'.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of Jihad and Jizya deals with the ruling on stipends ('ata) given from the public treasury. The author states that a stipend is considered a gift (sila) from the state, not a transfer of ownership before receipt. Therefore, the recipient does not become the owner until he actually takes possession (qabd). If the recipient dies before receiving the stipend, his right to it lapses; it does not become part of his estate. The text then specifies that in our time (the author's era), the typical recipients of such stipends are the judge (qadi), the mufti, and the teacher (mudarris). The commentary clarifies the implications: since the stipend is not owned before receipt, it cannot be inherited, sold, gifted, or divided among heirs prior to possession. This applies to all recurrent stipends, whether monthly or yearly. However, an exception is made for land grants: if the Imam gives land as absolute ownership (tamlik), then all legal consequences of ownership apply immediately, as explained by the scholar al-Qadi Muhammad in his work on land rulings. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for understanding the nature of state allocations.

Key Terms
عطاء (ʿaṭā): A stipend or grant from the public treasury (bayt al-mal) to eligible recipients, typically judges, muftis, and teachers.
صلة (ṣila): A gift or gratuity, not a contractual transfer of ownership; it is incomplete and revocable before delivery.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical possession or taking delivery, which is a condition for ownership in the case of stipends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a monthly stipend if the recipient dies before receiving it?
A) It becomes part of his inheritance.
B) It is cancelled and does not pass to his heirs.
C) It is given to his eldest son.
D) It is returned to the treasury with a penalty.

Key Takeaway
A stipend from the treasury is a revocable gift that does not confer ownership until possession; thus it cannot be inherited or transacted before receipt, except for land grants given as absolute ownership.

Paying the Apostate's Debts: Three Narrations
Ruling on which earnings pay debts incurred during Islam vs apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi narrations regarding payment of an apostate's debts, explain the preferred view using the maxim al-ghurm bi al-ghunm, and apply this ruling to a new scenario.

Core Idea
Liability follows benefit: debts are paid from earnings of the period when incurred.

How should the debts of an apostate be paid when he earned income both as a Muslim and as an apostate? The Hanafi school presents three narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. In this lesson, we examine the matn and commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, focusing on the reasoning behind the preferred view that each debt is paid from the earnings of its corresponding state, based on the maxim 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' (loss is proportionate to gain).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three narrations
Extract the three positions from Abu Hanifa: (1) each debt from its own period, (2) start with Islam earnings then supplement, (3) start with apostasy earnings then supplement.
The first narration is from al-Hidayah and is the preferred view.
2
Understand the basis of the first narration
The first narration ties each debt to the earnings of the period because the claimant (creditor) and the cause (contract) are different. This is supported by the maxim 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm'.
The maxim links liability to the benefit received from the earnings.
3
Analyze the other two narrations
The second and third narrations prioritize one period's earnings over the other, suggesting a different view on how to handle insufficiency. They imply a cross-subsidization.
These narrations are less relied upon in fatwa.
4
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Consider a case where an apostate earned only during Islam or only during apostasy. Apply the rule: debts are paid from the available earnings, but if debts exceed earnings, what happens?
The maxim ensures fairness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضي دين كل حال من كسب تلك) : أي دين حال الإسلام يقضى من كسب حال الإسلام، ودين حال الردة من كسب حال الردة. [2] قوله : أي دين حال الإسلام...الخ؛ قال في الهداية: هذه رواية عن أبي حنيفة، وعنه: إنه يبدأ بكسب الإسلام وإن لم يف بذلك يقضي من كسب الردة، وعنه: عكسه. وجه الأول: إن المستحق بالسببين مختلف، وحصول كل واحد من الكسبين باعتبار السبب الذي وجب له الدين، فيقضى كل دين من الكسب المكتسب الذي في تلك الحالة؛ ليكون الغرم بالغنم.
And the debt of each state is paid from the earnings of that state: meaning the debt of the state of Islam is paid from the earnings of the state of Islam, and the debt of the state of apostasy from the earnings of the state of apostasy. [2] His saying: i.e., the debt of the state of Islam... etc.; he said in al-Hidayah: This is a narration from Abu Hanifa, and from him (another narration): that he begins with the earnings of Islam, and if that is insufficient, it is paid from the earnings of apostasy, and from him (a third): the opposite. The basis of the first: that the claimant by two causes is different, and each of the two earnings is obtained by the cause for which the debt was owed, so each debt is paid from the earnings earned in that state so that loss is proportionate to gain.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage addresses the payment of debts owed by an apostate. The matn states that a debt incurred during Islam is paid from Islam earnings, and a debt during apostasy from apostasy earnings. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya then presents three narrations from Abu Hanifa on the order of payment. The first narration (from al-Hidayah) is that each debt is paid from its corresponding earnings. The second narration starts with Islam earnings and uses apostasy earnings if insufficient. The third narration reverses the order (start with apostasy earnings). The preferred view (first narration) is that each debt is paid from the earnings of the same period because the claimant (the creditor) and the cause (the contract) are different for each period, and the principle is that loss follows gain (al-ghurm bi al-ghunm).

Key Terms
دين (dayn): A debt or financial obligation incurred by the apostate during a specific state (Islam or apostasy).
كسب (kasb): Earnings acquired during a specific period (Islam or apostasy).
الغرم بالغنم (al-ghurm bi al-ghunm): A legal maxim: loss is proportionate to gain (liability follows benefit). Here, it justifies paying debts from the earnings of the period in which the debt was incurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi narration, how should an apostate's debts be paid?
A) All debts are paid first from Islam earnings.
B) Each debt is paid from the earnings of the period in which it was incurred.
C) All debts are paid from apostasy earnings first.
D) Debts are paid proportionally from both earnings combined.

Key Takeaway
Debts of an apostate are paid from the earnings of the period in which they were incurred, because liability follows benefit (al-ghurm bi al-ghunm).

Debts of the Apostate: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Views on Earnings
Explains how to pay debts of an apostate from his earnings during Islam vs apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi mufta bihi position on paying the debts of an apostate from his earnings during Islam and apostasy, and compare it with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
The apostate's debts are paid from both Islamic and apostasy earnings collectively, as per the Sahibayn, because he retains property rights despite apostasy.

When a Muslim apostatizes, his legal capacity for ownership becomes ambiguous. How should his debts be settled from his earnings before and after apostasy? This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab differences, clarifying the operative ruling used in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: from which of the apostate's earnings (Islamic period or apostasy period) should his debts be paid?
This affects the distribution to heirs.
2
Recognize the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all apostate property becomes fay' (treasury) or ownerless, so no inheritance for Muslim heirs.
Two sayings: fay' or ownerless.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's three narrations
Abu Hanifa has three reported views: (1) segregate debts by earnings period, (2) prioritize Islamic earnings, (3) prioritize apostasy earnings.
These are all within the Hanafi school but not mufta bihi.
4
Grasp the Sahibayn's unified view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that both earnings are the apostate's property, so debts are paid from the total estate, and heirs inherit the remainder.
This is the zahir al-riwayah and mufta bihi.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The preferred Hanafi position is that all debts are paid from the combined earnings of both periods, then heirs inherit.
This is more practical and treats the apostate's property as unified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وقضي دين كل حال من كسب تلك) : أي دين حال الإسلام" يقضى من كسب حال الإسلام، ودين حال الردة من كسب حال الردة [1] قوله : كلاهما فيء ؛ فيوضع في بيت المال، ويصرف في مصارف الجزية والخراج هذا على أحد قوليه وفي قوله الآخر : يوضع فيه بطريق أنه مال ضائع. ووجه قوله : إن المرتد كافر، والمسلم لا يرث الكافر، فلا يكون شيء من ماله لوارثه المسلم ، فيكون كلّه فيئاً ، نعم ؛ لو كان له وارث ارتد معه ورثه. ولهما : إن المرتد يجبر على ردّه إلى الإسلام، فيحكم عليه في حق ورثته بأحكامه، فكلا الكسبين ملك له، ولهذا تقضى ديونه منهما ، فكلاهما يكون لورثته. ولأبي حنيفة له : الفرق بين كسبيه : بأنّ حكم موته يستند إلى وقت ردّته ؛ لأنه صار هالكاً بالردّة، فيمكن استناد التوريث فيما كسبه حال إسلامه إلى قبيل ذلك الوقت ؛ لأنه كان موجوداً في ملكه حينئذ فيكون توريثاً للمسلم من المسلم، ولا يمكن فيما اكتسبه في حال ردّته أن يسند توريثه إلى زمان إسلامه ؛ إذ لم يكن موجوداً في ملكه في ذلك الزمان، فلو قضى به لورثته لكان توريثاً للمسلم من الكافر فلا يجوز، كذا في «شرح الفرائض الشريفية» (۲) للسيد الشريف علي الجُرْجَانِي وغيره. (٢) قوله : أي دين حال الإسلام...الخ ؛ قال في «الهداية»(۳) : «هذه رواية عن أبي حنيفة ، وعنه : إنّه يبدأ بكسب الإسلام وإن لم يف بذلك يقضي من كسب الردّة، وعنه : عكسه. وجه الأول : إنّ المستحق بالسببين مختلف، وحصول كل واحد من الكسبين باعتبار السبب الذي وجب له الدين، فيقضى كلّ دين من الكسب المكتسب الذي في تلك الحالة ؛ ليكون الغرم بالغنم. وجه الثاني : إن كسب الإسلام ملكه حتى يخلفه الوارث فيه، ومن شرط هذه الخلافة الفراغ عن حق الوارث، فيقدّم الدِّين عليهم. أما كسب الردّة فليس بمملوك له لبطلان أهلية الملك بالردّة عنده، فلا يقضى دينه منه إلا إذا تعذر قضاؤه من محل آخر، فحينئذ يقضى منه كالذمي إذا مات ولا وارث له يكون ماله الجماعة المسلمين، ولو كان عليه دين يقضى منه ، كذا هاهنا. وجه الثالث : إنّ كسب الإسلام حق الورثة ، وكسب الردّة خالص حقه، فكان قضاء الدين منه أولى إلا إذا تعذر إن لم يف به ، فحينئذ تقضى من كسب الإسلام. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : تقضى ديونه من الكسبين؛ لأنهما جميعاً ملكه حتى يجري الإرث فيهما». انتهى.
(And the debt of each condition is paid from the earnings of that condition): meaning, the debt of the condition of Islam is paid from the earnings of the condition of Islam, and the debt of the condition of apostasy is paid from the earnings of the condition of apostasy. [1] His statement: both are fay' (booty); so they are placed in the treasury and spent on the expenditures of jizya and kharaj. This is according to one of his (al-Shafi'i's) two sayings. In his other saying: it is placed there as a form of ownerless property. The proof of his statement: the apostate is an unbeliever, and a Muslim does not inherit from an unbeliever, so nothing of his wealth goes to his Muslim heir, thus all of it is fay'. Yes, if he had an heir who apostatized with him, that heir inherits from him. And for him (Abu Hanifa): there is a difference between his two earnings: the rule of his death is linked to the time of his apostasy, because he became destroyed by apostasy. So it is possible to attribute inheritance of what he earned during his Islam to just before that time, because it existed in his ownership at that time, so it would be inheritance of a Muslim from a Muslim. It is not possible for what he earned during his apostasy to attribute its inheritance to the time of his Islam, because it did not exist in his ownership at that time. So if it were given to his heirs, it would be inheritance of a Muslim from an unbeliever, which is not permissible. Thus in Sharh al-Fara'id al-Sharifiyya and others. (2) His statement: 'debt of the condition of Islam...etc.'; he said in al-Hidayah: 'This is a narration from Abu Hanifa, and from him (another narration): that he begins with the earnings of Islam, and if that is insufficient, then from the earnings of apostasy; and from him (another): the opposite. The justification of the first: the debts are different due to different causes, and each earning is acquired due to the cause for which the debt was incurred, so each debt is paid from the earning acquired in that condition, so that loss is proportionate to gain. The justification of the second: the earnings of Islam are his property such that the heir succeeds him in it, and a condition of this succession is freedom from the right of the heirs, so debts are given priority over them. As for the earnings of apostasy, they are not his property due to the nullification of the capacity of ownership by apostasy in his view, so his debt is not paid from it unless it is impossible to pay from another source; then it is paid from it, like the dhimmi who dies without an heir, his wealth goes to the Muslim community, and if he had a debt, it is paid from it. Likewise here. The justification of the third: the earnings of Islam are the right of the heirs, and the earnings of apostasy are purely his right, so paying the debt from the latter is more appropriate, unless it is insufficient, then it is paid from the earnings of Islam.' And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: his debts are paid from both earnings, because both are his property such that inheritance occurs in both.' End.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about how to satisfy the debts of an apostate (murtadd). The core issue: from which of his assets (earned during Islam or during apostasy) should his debts be paid? The text presents multiple views. The main positions: (1) Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all property of the apostate becomes fay' for the Muslim treasury, because a Muslim cannot inherit from an apostate. So debts are paid from the treasury? Actually, the text mentions two sayings: either both earnings are fay' and used for jizya/kharaj, or they are treated as ownerless property (mal da'i'). (2) Abu Hanifa is reported in at least three narrations: (a) each debt is paid from the earnings of the same condition (Islamic debts from Islamic earnings, apostasy debts from apostasy earnings); (b) begin with Islamic earnings, then if insufficient, use apostasy earnings; (c) the opposite: begin with apostasy earnings. (3) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold that both earnings are his property and debts are paid from both proportionally; his heirs then inherit the remainder. The commentator explains the reasoning behind each view. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is not explicitly stated here, but in the Hanafi school, the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) is often preferred in financial matters. The key principle: the apostate's legal capacity is affected; his Islamic earnings are treated as Muslim-owned, while apostasy earnings are not fully his property due to his loss of capacity to own (according to Abu Hanifa). The text also references al-Hidayah for the multiple narrations from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Booty or property taken from non-Muslims without fighting, which goes to the public treasury. Here, the apostate's property is considered fay' by al-Shafi'i.
مرتد (murtadd): A Muslim who leaves Islam. The text discusses his legal rulings regarding property, debts, marriage, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, how are the debts of an apostate paid?
A) Debts are paid only from earnings during Islam
B) Debts are paid only from earnings during apostasy
C) Debts are paid from both Islamic and apostasy earnings collectively
D) Debts are not paid; all property goes to the treasury

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the apostate's debts are settled from his total estate, regardless of when the earnings were acquired, ensuring creditors are paid and heirs inherit the remainder.

Rulings on the Apostate's Marriage, Slaughter, Divorce, and Paternity
States rulings on four actions of an apostate with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the marriage, slaughter, divorce, and istilad of an apostate, and explain the legal reasoning based on the concepts of millah and legal capacity.

Core Idea
The apostate's marriage and slaughter are invalid due to lack of millah, but his divorce and istilad remain valid as they do not require full religious affiliation or ownership.

The apostate (murtadd) occupies a unique legal position in Hanafi fiqh: having left Islam, some of his actions are rendered invalid because they depend on a recognized religious community (millah). However, other actions that do not require such a basis or require only partial legal capacity remain valid. This lesson unpacks these rulings and the reasoning behind them, including a classic objection and its response.

Guided Path
1
State the four rulings
Identify the two invalid actions (marriage and slaughter) and the two valid actions (divorce and istilad) of an apostate as per the Hanafi school.
Marriage and slaughter are batil; divorce and istilad are sahih.
2
Understand the role of millah for marriage and slaughter
Marriage and slaughter require a valid religious community (millah) to be considered legitimate. The apostate has no millah, so his marriage and slaughter are invalid.
Millah here means a recognized religion under which marriage produces inheritance and procreation.
3
Analyze the objection and answer regarding millah
An objector points out that Jews and Christians have valid marriages and slaughter without being Muslim, and polytheists have valid marriages without a revealed religion. The answer refines the definition of millah: it is a religion whose marriages are recognized for inheritance and procreation. Apostates are excluded because the male is killed and the female imprisoned.
The objection tests the precise meaning of millah.
4
Explain the rationale for divorce and istilad
Divorce is valid because it does not require complete legal authority (wilayah); it is valid from a slave, so more so from an apostate. Istilad (establishing paternity) is valid because it does not require actual ownership; it can be done with a son's slave-girl, so it is valid for an apostate who owns a slave-girl.
The reasoning uses qiyas: the apostate is compared to a slave or the case of a son's slave-girl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وبطل نكاحه وذبحه، وصح طلاقه واستيلاده) [۱] قوله : وبطل نكاحه وذبحه ؛ لأنهما يعتمدان قيام الملة، ولا ملة للمرتد. وأورد عليه : بأن الملة إن كان المراد بها الإسلام انتقض بنكاح أهل الكتاب وذبائحهم، فإنهما صحيحان مع عدم الإسلام، وإن كان المراد بها الملة السماوية مطلقاً انتقض بنكاح المجوس والمشركين فيما بينهم، فإنّه صحيح ولا ملة سماوية لهم. وأجيب عنه : بأن المراد بالملة ما يتدينون به نكاحاً يقرون عليه، بحيث يجري التوارث بينهما، ويحصل الغرض من النكاح من التوالد والتناسل، والمرتدان ليسا بهذه المثابة ، فإنّ المرتد يقتل والمرتدة تحبس. [۲] قوله : وصح ؛ أما صحة الاستيلاد ؛ فلأنه لا يفتقر إلى حقيقة الملك بدليل أنه يصح في جارية الابن ؛ فلأن يصح من المرتد ،أولى، وصورتُه : أن تجيء أمته بولد فيدعيه يثبت نسبه منه، ويرث ذلك الولد منه مع ورثته، وتصير الجارية أم ولد له، وأما صحة الطلاق ؛ فلأنه لا يفتقر إلى قيام الولاية الكاملة، بدليل أنه يصح من العبد ؛ فلأن يصح من المرتد أولى، كذا في «البحر» وغيره.
(And his marriage and his slaughter are invalid, and his divorce and his establishing paternity are valid). [1] His statement: his marriage and his slaughter are invalid because they depend on the existence of a religious law (millah), and the apostate has no religious law. An objection was raised: if by religious law is meant Islam, then this is contradicted by the marriage and slaughter of the People of the Book, for they are valid despite the absence of Islam. If by religious law is meant any revealed religion, then it is contradicted by the marriage of Magians and polytheists among themselves, for it is valid although they have no revealed religion. The answer: what is meant by religious law is that by which they conduct their marriages that are recognized, such that inheritance occurs between them, and the purpose of marriage (procreation) is achieved. But the two apostates are not in this situation, because the male apostate is killed and the female apostate is imprisoned. [2] His statement: and it is valid; as for the validity of establishing paternity (istilad), it is because it does not require actual ownership, evidenced by the fact that it is valid in the case of the son's slave-girl; so it is all the more valid from the apostate. Its scenario: his slave-girl gives birth, he claims the child, the child's lineage is established from him, that child inherits from him along with his other heirs, and the slave-girl becomes an umm walad for him. As for the validity of divorce, it is because it does not require complete authority (wilayah), evidenced by the fact that it is valid from a slave; so it is all the more valid from the apostate. Thus in al-Bahr and others.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof regarding the validity of certain actions by an apostate. The text first states the ruling: the apostate's marriage and slaughter (dhabh) are invalid, while his divorce (talaq) and establishing paternity (istilad) are valid. The commentator then provides the reasoning and defends against objections. For marriage and slaughter: they depend on a 'millah' (religious law/community). The apostate has no millah because he has left Islam and is considered without a recognized religion. An objection arises: if millah means Islam, then how are the marriages and slaughter of Jews and Christians valid? Also, if millah means any revealed religion, how are marriages of polytheists valid? The answer: millah here means a religion under which marriage is recognized for the purpose of inheritance and procreation. Apostates are not allowed to continue their marriage because the male is to be executed and the female imprisoned, so their marriage does not serve these purposes. For istilad (establishing paternity of a child born to his slave-girl): this is valid because it does not require full ownership; it is valid even if the slave-girl is owned by someone else (like the son's slave-girl). So it is valid from the apostate since he still owns the slave-girl. For divorce: it does not require complete legal authority (wilayah kamilah); it is valid from a slave, so it is valid from an apostate too. These rulings are from the Hanafi school. The source mentioned is al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
ملة (millah): Religious law or community that forms the basis for valid marriage and slaughter. The apostate lacks this.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master claiming paternity of a child born to his slave-girl, which establishes lineage and makes the slave-girl an umm walad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the divorce of an apostate valid despite his apostasy?
A) Because divorce requires complete authority, which the apostate still has
B) Because divorce does not require complete authority, similar to the validity of a slave's divorce
C) Because the apostate is considered a Muslim for legal purposes
D) Because divorce is a financial transaction

Key Takeaway
The apostate's marriage and slaughter are invalid due to lack of millah, but his divorce and istilad remain valid because they do not require complete authority or actual ownership.
